0553801473|2.0|I'm beginning this \\"review\\"(although it is more accurately a letter of complaint) before I've finished the book. At this point I'm 62 chapters in, and 81% done, and I'm sick to death of it. I'm struggling to finish, and I never not finish books. The only book I ever didn't finish in disgust was the first Wraethlu book and this is nearly as bad as that. I don't ever remember waiting this long for a book and being so disappointed. Even the Deathly Hallows didn't disappoint me as much as this, and that--if you'll remember my horror at that book--is saying something.<br /><br />I have now finished and while the book took a minor turn for the better it soon plummeted back into \\"disappointing.\\"<br />The main problem to me is that it's bloated beyond control. I can't blame the editors for this, because I understand they had very little time to manage a monster of a book and it had already been cut down by about a third, apparently, but there's so much padding it suffocates under its own weight, like Lord Manderley falling asleep on his wife, or something.<br /><br />Plus I would suspect that the editors worked on an arc, or a pov, and didn't see the book as a whole--but even if that was the case, there's no excuse for the repetition and rehashing Facts are repeated within the same POV - we are told a dozen (maybe two dozen) times how Tyrion killed his father and Shae, as if we'd forgotten, not only since reading the book where that happened, but since reading Tyrion's previous chapter. We are reminded, over and over and over and in fact, over, who is leal to Winterfell and who has turned their cloak and who are enemies--so many times that I just wanted to scream. Even Asha, who in previous books knows nothing of the grass-soft lords and their history, suddenly seems an expert and we are reminded YET AGAIN what the position is regarding the Karstarks.<br /><br />Every time we see Davos (and as much as I like Davos as a character I was glad we didn't see him more than we did or I would have given up reading altogether) we are reminded over and over (and indeed, over) and over again that he had seven sons and he's lost four and about his fingers.and every single thing else we've ever learned about this insignificant character. It's not as if this fact is pigging relevant. Then, when we are invested with all this time with Davos, he's dropped and we don't see where he goes, who he's with, blah de blah de blah. He's a deus ex machina, simply there to show plot points as they move on.<br /><br />So many chapters are not only rehashing, \\"as you know, Bob\\" narration and dialogue, but are entirely unnecessary. It seems that GRRM thought \\"well, x person has got to get to x place so I'd better show the journey.\\" And the actual journeys are pointless.<br /><br />Seriously. Tyrion's entire story of hundreds of pages could have started \\"Tyrion woke up and found he didn't like being a slave.\\" Yes,there was one vital character he met on his travels, but his interaction with him was inconsequential and we did need the whole journey and the turtles. Ditto for Quentyn's journey. Ditto for Victarian's journey.<br /><br />This is a classic example of his padding (in miniature)<br /><br />\\"the sky above Meereen was the colour of corpse flesh, dull and white and heavy, a mass of unbroken cloud from horizon to horizon. The sun was hidden behind a wall of cloud. it would set unseen, as it had risen unseen that morning.\\"<br /><br />You see what I mean. We've had a literary description of the wall of cloud in teh sky, then for people who don't quite get it, we get \\"it's cloudy\\" Then for the complete thickos we are told what the consequence of this will be.<br /><br />I am not judging the book on this one section, merely holding it up as a classic example of GRRM taking two or three ways to say the same thing. I have been tweeting over and over that \\"nothing has happened yet\\" and while that's not entirely true, it certainly felt like it--probably due to the over-weighted and bloated chapters that are nothing more than description and travelogue and internal (repetitious) dialogue and angsting and telling us crap we already know and listing everyone in the scene and every merchant and every turtle and every type of food and every mercenary<br /><br />I have learned that - if you can take out a scene, or a chapter entirely and it makes no difference at all to the plot--then that scene is entirely irrelevant and should--must--be excised. Now, of course, I don't know GRRMS plan, it might be that there's a very good reason he left all the boring tripe in, but it didn't help to make this the kind of book that the first 3 were.<br /><br />The problem with the chapters that are (probably) essential is everyone's so bloody PASSIVE. There's a reason Jon is, so that didn't bother me, but what the hell happened to Dany and Tyrion? Did they both have lobotomies somewhere between books 4 and 5? Dany has gone down with a case of Tonks-itis. (Tonks, from Harry Potter was a smart, feisty auror -that alone meant she was in the top 3percent of her class--who rocked in the fighting department.She was clever, witty and amusing. Then in later books she fell in lurve, lost her powers, became nothing more than a weepy clinging woman and consequently died. Even when we see Ghost!Remus later on, she's not with him.) And Dany has gone the same way. She won a Horse Lord's heart, and his followers, and thousands of poorfolk's hearts, then turns into nothing more than a girl in love led by men.<br /><br />Love doesn't do well in this series,as a matter of course. Of course I know that the good side doesn't always win, but sheesh - sometimes they do. But there's no character here who falls in love who gets a fair deal. In fact, GRRM goes so far to list a whole heap of people who married for love and allthe appalling things that happened to them. (that's on top of the main characters who married for love and died horribly. Cat/Ned, Lysa, Dany/Drogo, Tyrion/Tysha and the others who loved without marriage and died horribly... Add to that the sudden homophobia from all corners,when it had been much more accepted before. No, love doesn't do well.<br /><br />LISTs: What was the point of all the lists? Naming every hostage that came through the wall? All the irritating hostages at irritating Meereen (is there a point to Meereen?) All with too many Zs in their names.<br /><br />There were (a paltry few) chapters I liked. Perhaps ten out of 70 PLUS. But they don't save this book which could have been cut in half and wouldn't have lost a thing.<br /><br />I will not be buying the next one. 0439023513|2.0|\\"Mocking Jay\\" by Suzanne Collins, was long-anticipated by me, and ultimately the most difficult of the trilogy for me to read.  Plot Spoilers  I enjoyed reading \\"Hunger Games\\" despite my 2 great reservations about such a book.  Throughout Book 1 and the series, I did not buy into the concept of a sort of Roman ampitheatre games.  It seems to me personally (and I don't expect others to agree with me) that:  i) sacrificing children and teens by adults is a bit of a cheap exploit sometimes used by writers (I didn't enjoy the concept in \\"His Dark Materials\\" by Philip Pullman either).    ii) getting characters to kill others as a form of survival is a dark and potentially-unenjoyable plot device. Not even that original as it turns out.  The plot twists at the denouement of \\"Hunger Games\\" and \\"Catching Fire\\" made them memorable. The humanity, compassion and rebelliousness of Katniss, Peeta, Gale and the wide cast of characters is what kept me going to the end.  However, unfortunately, I found the key ingredients of a humane and an adversarial element missing in Book #3.  It made the experience of reading it wearisome, like wading through the muddy banks of a river to get into the water. It took me an amazing 2 weeks to read \\"Mockingjay\\", when normally I would have chopped through at least 2 books in that time. The ending didn't wash away the muddy deposits for me; it didn't alleviate the horror I felt while reading the book.  It is not exactly fun for me to read about the nightmare of killing others, and then the protagonist having nightmares about it. So this review might not resonate with those who genuinely enjoy reading dystopic books. I do enjoy reading about a dystopia, as long as there is some leavening of the experience - whether it is in the humanity of the characters, or the occasional use of humour, or the occasional win by the protagonists.  There were far too few of the leavening elements for my enjoyment.  Other quibbles:  1. Katniss' personality was not realised for me. Was she a rebel leader, or a teen girl with all the limitations of understanding the wider political situation?  I thought the former at the beginning of the book, then veered towards the latter explanation through most of Mockingjay, until the tension and her personality is somewhat alleviated by her action towards Coin at the end. The end was not enough to persuade me to like the book, either in terms of Katniss' role in the book or of the storyline.  2. The victors' and the make up artists' fates were not realistic to me. It hadn't been really shown up in Book 2, \\"Playing with Fire\\", so it felt like a plot device to shock. Instead of making me want to read further, it felt like all the odds were too stacked in the Capital's favour.  As a reader, I want to feel like the protagonists in which I have invested my time and interest, have some pros working for them.  3. Ditto Peeta, re #2. His personality changes - from negative to positive to negative to positive - were not convincing.  4. How did Prim even make it to the Capital, let alone be a burning bird? It would have been helpful to have had some explanation that she had made it there. Instead, I thought Katniss was seeing her own recollection of Rue amongst the burning children.  Chilling, disturbing and without the requisite salve during it and at the end to make it as worthwhile for me as books 1 and 2 of this trilogy were.

It's a major struggle trying to concentrate through intense heat.  Oh well.  I do actually enjoy this album a heck of a lot more this morning with a fresher perspective and 9 hours of much needed sleep.      I still feel Jefferson Airplane/Jefferson Starship were much much better songwriting-wise, but the entire first side of Blows Against the Empire for example really improved for me *dramatically* this morning.  I suppose I can look at this Paul Kantner album as being more on the hypnotic/dreamy side of the Jefferson Airplane/Jefferson Starship adventure, or perhaps a journey in between the two incarnations of the band.  Either way, I'm really happy I'm able to enjoy it now because I have WAY too much respect and admiration for Paul Kantner's singing abilities to simply write him off the way I did last night.    I originally said \\"Mau Mau\\" was boring and well, a part of me still thinks it is, but the vocal melody *has* grown on me a heck of a lot more all of a sudden.  I can't explain how a rambling mess turned into gold but somehow, it did.  That never happened with any Jefferson Airplane song for example (though it *did* happen with a few Jefferson Starship songs and I imagine if, for example, I had heard \\"Miracles\\" for the first time last night considering the miserable way I was feeling, I'd consider that masterpiece of a song a rambling mess too).  \\"Let's Go Together\\" is a great pop song with... is that a banjo in the background? Something feels missing with this album.  Actually there's at least two huge problems.  For one, Paul Kantner's vocals sound extremely forced.  It's like Paul Kantner is trying desperately to capture the crazy/unique atmosphere of a Jefferson Airplane album but never able to achieve it.  He never comes close.  \\"Mau Mau\\" is almost unlistenable as a result.   His vocals on this track are unbearable because he apparently forgot which vocal melodies are more worthy than others so he just continues to sing hoping something eventually sticks but to my displeasure, nothing ever does.  At least, not from where I stand.    Furthermore, his vocals sound flat and uninspired.  It's like this for the entire album.  Kantner, nor does the rest of the band, ever do anything remotely interesting to help move his vocals along in an exciting way, so his vocal melodies end up falling apart after a few minutes when it becomes apparent that his voice is being used for the sole purpose of dragging endlessly.  This particular complaint of mine continues for the entire album and it's downright surprising to me because this was *never* a complaint on any Jefferson Airplane/Jefferson Starship album.  On those albums I figured the guy was such a genius that he'd find a way to make his style work no matter what.  Sadly, that's not the case and the guy's human after all.      The songwriting is alarmingly ordinary thanks to the way Paul Kantner sings the vocal melodies.  It's like he doesn't really care and he honestly sounds drunk and unaware how embarrassing he sounds.  Trust me, I absolutely love this guy any other time and believe he has one of the nicest voices in rock music, but for some unexplainable reason it's an incredibly tough task hearing his vocal work on Blows Against the Empire.      Any guitar solos are really simplistic and restrained and never able to make me feel entertained unlike his previous tenure with Jefferson Airplane.  None of these guitar solos do anything interesting at all.  They're just there for the sake of making an appearance.  I truly don't understand the appeal here, I'm afraid.  An extremely disappointing album.    \\"Hijack\\" is only memorable because the verse melody sounds remarkably similar to the one from Jefferson Airplane's \\"We Can Live Together\\".  \\"A Child Is Coming\\" has a pretty good bouncy rhythm section with nice vocals giving it a semi-emotional feeling before quickly turning into a disappointing piano-based jam with overly dramatic vocals that, again, drag for an aggravatingly extended period of time.  These two songs provide the only compliments I can give to this album.      Nowadays it seems most people prefer this Paul Kantner album over the first four Jefferson Starship albums and several Jefferson Airplane albums.  I truly fail to see why.  I think music listeners got it right back in the day- this stuff is mediocre.  This album sorely needed direction along with strong and sincere vocal melodies because that's what I've come to love about Kantner.  Whatever he was attempting to do here is a failed experiment.

B004Y1USJE|4.0|There is so much to say about Yes' Fly From Here album. Regardless of wherever your loyalties lie with any particular member of Yes, past or present, give the album a chance.    The 23-minute side-long \\"Fly From Here\\" epic has so much going on, it feels like the shortest 23 minutes you'll experience. The piano chords in the overture marks the return of Geoff Downes to Yes since leaving in 1980. When the band slams in with the short, sharp blasts of fury, then, if you're familiar at all with Yes' 1980 album Drama, you will recognize how similar those blasts are to Drama's opening track \\"Machine Messiah.\\" Benoit David's vocals come across as an eerie hybrid of Jon Anderson and Trevor Horn at first, but then by about the 9-minute mark he takes on a Midge Ure (lead singer of Ultravox) quality and maintains that the rest of the song. If you have been jonesing for new Pink Floyd music, then \\"Fly From Here Part III\\" might fill that void through Steve Howe's slide guitar solo on top of Horn's and Downe's atmospheric organ and synthesizers and drummer Alan White's slow groove. My only complaint is that \\"Part V\\" wants to be this triumphant restatement of the chorus at the end, but the transition from the quirky double-time \\"Part IV\\" to the bombast feels awkward. The whole song comes across to me as being the last dance number from a stage musical version of Casablanca, set on the tarmac and Benoit is Rick Blaine singing \\"Here's looking at you, kid\\" to Ilsa Lund.    \\"The Man You Always Wanted Me To Be\\" opens side two with Yes mainstay member Chris Squire on lead vocals. This song wouldn't be out of place on a 1990's Yes album like Talk or Open Your Eyes. It is in this song, the vocal alterations (possibly Autotune pitch correction) become fairly evident. The vocals are all too perfect-sounding, but, then again, when you remember that Yes was never a band to shun new technology, this move can be heard as much as an artistic decision as a corrective measure. Yes songs used Vocoders back in the day, too. This song also has one of the stronger hooks, musically. Lyrically, it's one of the most down-to-earth personal songs by Yes.    \\"Life On a Film Set\\" is another so-called \\"leftover\\" from 1980 (\\"Fly From Here\\" was also originally written in 1980). It was at this point I noticed that Downes' choices of synthesizer sounds and keyboards throughout the album recall the late '70s and early '80s. He really is picking up where he left off in 1980 using the same keyboards! You may think the line \\"riding the tiger\\" is silly at first, and you may never get over it, but --jeez-- when was the last time Yes released a song with a sense of humor?    \\"Hour of Need\\" is the song for all those who would miss Jon Anderson. It is very easy to imagine Jon singing this call-to-action message song, and it's evident here how Benoit's mimicry talent landed him his previous gig singing in a top-notch Yes cover band. As a song, it doesn't have a very strong melody or memorable lyrics ... pretty much like a song Jon might have written as of late (zing!).    \\"Solitaire\\" is Steve Howe's acoustic guitar showcase. It's quite beautiful. In fact, Steve's performance on the entire album is signature Steve. Steve commands more space on this album than Downe's keyboards, Squire's bass, White's drums, and even David's voice. If you are a fan of Steve Howe, this is an album you need to hear. His trademark lead guitar lines are here. While the speed and stamina has slowed down a touch, he more than compensates with taste and flair on the entire album.    \\"Into the Storm\\" immediately recalls the 1969-70 Yes, if you can believe that. Listening to this song reminds me of \\"Something's Coming,\\" \\"Sweet Dreams\\" and, especially \\"Survival\\" (because of Squire's wah-wah bass guitar). If you're familiar at all with very early Yes, then this song will surely make you smile, letting you know that while you may think that by regrouping the Drama band Yes is going too far back in time, Yes can goback much further than that. It is also on this last song of the album that Benoit sounds most like Jon Anderson.    Lastly, I bought this album on vinyl and feel compelled to forewarn anyone considering the vinyl version that the album is about 48 minutes long, 23 minutes on side one and about 25 minutes on side two. These lengths are not conducive to a particular high quality, because the grooves are packed tighter together. On my copy on each side, beginning about 13 minutes into the side, the high end begins to lightly \\"crackle.\\" This could be because of any number of reasons, like my copy was near the end of a pressing run or careless lacquer cutting. It doesn't interfere too much in my enjoyment of the music, but to some other audiophiles it might.    Fly From Here is a worthy addition to the Yes discography. It doesn't break any new ground, but it does do what Yes does best. Maybe \\"classic progressive rock\\" should be the new moniker for this genre.    In the end, it sounds here like Yes is having fun being a band and just rocking out for the sake of just rocking out (and being a little silly -- intentionally) without the burden of delivering \\"the big statement.\\"

B000002LGA|5.0|I just broke this album out today -- not only because it's long been a favorite but because it's 100 degrees here in New York City, I hadn't listened to this in awhile and I thought it would be a great backdrop for a day like this. I was right.    As many people have said, this was one of several career highs for Lou Reed. Released as the 1980s came to an end, the album \\"New York\\" sums up that decade, both in NYC and, to a lesser degree, in America at large. Listening to this album a little more than 20 years later (!), it's amazing how great it still sounds. I think there are two reasons. One is musical: for most songs, Reed employs a simple lineup of two guitars, bass and drums. This provides an effective and minimal rock and roll approach for his songs and it also pushes the lyrics to the forefront.     It's those lyrics that really make this a great disc, though! There are few if any weak songs on \\"New York.\\" For an hour, Reed complains about the downhill slide of Manhattan, AIDS, poverty, animals that are becoming extinct, violence (both inner city and domestic), religion, greed, relationships, Jesse Jackson and other subjects. You can accuse him of being a pessimist but the fact is, Reed's complaints and observations are generally right on. What's amazing when you listen to the album now is how far ahead of his time the guy was! In \\"Hold On,\\" Reed sings \\"The haves and have-nots are bleeding in the tub/That's New York's future, not mine.\\" Of course, his prediction was accurate since by 2011 (which is when I'm writing this review), the middle class in Manhattan is nearly non-existent. Similarly, in  the mellower \\"Last Great American Whale,\\" he addresses that mammal's imminent extinction. While I have no idea where whales stand now with regard to becoming extinct. there's no question that since \\"New York\\" was released, things have gone to hell environmentally, between global warming and some species of animals dying out.     For most of this album, Reed points his fingers at more general subjects, but when he gets specific his lyrics hit even closer to home. In \\"Good Evening Mr. Waldheim,\\" he goes after Jesse Jackson, accusing the politician of racism -- a ballsy move but Reed is no racist and he knows that the truth is not always politically correct. (For what it's worth, my best friend, who happens to be black, loves that tune and this whole album.)  The best line in the song may be \\"If I ran for president/And once was a member of the Klan/Wouldn't you call me on it?/ The way I call you on Farrakhan.\\" On a more sensitive note, the song \\"Dime Store Mystery,\\" which closes the album, is about the death of Reed's close friend and mentor, Andt Warhol. It ends things on a chilling, nagging note.     The centerpiece of \\"New York\\" is probably \\"Busload of Faith.\\" A great song, in which Reed lists all the things in life that you CAN'T depend on. These include friends, family, religion, the justice system, intelligence and God. \\"You can depend on the worst always happening,\\" he sing/speaks. \\"It takes a busload of faith to get by.\\" Rarely has disgust sounded so transcendent.

B00004VWAF|2.0|I write as a huge fan of Miles Davis' electric period. For me this is the most fascinating period of his music, 70's Miles is my favourite Miles decade - and my favourite late 20th century music too.      So I'd like to say, first of all, that the issue here has nothing to do with purism, \\"classic jazz\\" versus electric or \\"fusion\\", forget all this nonsense. The only problem with this album is simply that it isn't very good.    It stands oddly in the middle of the period, in 1972, between two peaks: The first peak being his studio work of 1969-70 which yielded In a Silent Way, Bitches Brew, Jack Johnson and the best part of Big Fun. The other peak is 1973-75, as documented on Get Up With It and Agharta/Pangea. All of those are absolutely monumental musical statements.    Recorded in-between, On the Corner was an experiment, an interesting experiment: to create a moving tapestry of rhythms and sounds. It certainly wasn't an attempt to \\"sell\\" to the pop market - the accusation i've frequently read about, and which I find totally ludicrous given the abstraction of the work.     As interesting as the concept was, it doesn't seem to get anywhere: the layers, the rhythms and the motifs simply pass by, at once too cluttered for ambiance and too loose for attentive listening. The album has been re-appraised as a forerunner of 90's modern dance styles: this could be a good thing, or a bad thing, depending on your perspective on said styles. When the album is over, I feel a sigh of relief coming - bad sign when I can't get enough of the other electric albums.    Interestingly the On the Corner sessions yielded the track \\"Ife\\", which Teo Macero used for the patchwork album Big Fun. \\"Ife\\" is actually a nicer piece than anything on On the Corner: a beguiling groove with something more melodic which dissolves into an ambient mood where Mutme's African water Drums mix with the sound of organ and wha-wha trumpet: that, actually, was pointing forward to Agharta and Pangea.    Still i'll be coming back to this album. It is, after all, electric Miles Davis.

Even with my point and shoot, exposure is something that I've chronically struggled with--and I know I can't be the only one! My photos are always under- or over-exposed, never in-between. I spent lots of time trying to correct this in various editing software during post-processing, when I just wish I could have gotten it RIGHT when I took the picture in the first place! This book really helped me understand the combination of ISO, aperture, and shutter speed, allowing me to not just take pictures that are no longer under- or over-exposed, but also convey the impression I wanted.    The layout of the book is confusing if you're looking for a reference guide where you can flip to a certain page, read a paragraph, and put the book away. That's only going to make sense if you're looking for a brief refresher because you already know the general information in the book. However, I did appreciate the way everything was arranged as someone who intended to (and did) read it cover to cover. Everything was clearly explained and the new content built on the old content. It reads as a cohesive whole, not as a choppy combination of notes. The focus on concepts, rather than specific settings, makes the information easy to apply to any camera regardless of make or model. For example, even though I read this to improve my skills with a DSLR, I've used the same information to take better pictures with my point and shoot. While the Fly From Here suite of songs makes an attempt to harken back to the days of Close to the Edge and Tales from Topographic Oceans, it simply lacks the soaring melodies and intensity of YES's mid- to late-1970s work.  David Benoit's voice, while solid, makes me greater appreciate Jon Anderson's falsetto vocals.  Solitaire is a traditional Steve Howe solo spot that is on par with Masquerade from Union, although not comparable to The Clap.  The Man You Always Wanted Me To Be, Life On A Film Set, and Hour Of Need do not contain any memorable riffs or lyrics and are boring.  The album's finale, Into The Storm, attempts to deliver a fast-paced, Drama-style pseudo-progressive sound, but it is too little, too late.  None of the songs on Fly From Here delivers a progressive sound, but none of the songs delivers a hit like the Cinema-era YES did with Owner of a Lonely Heart and Changes.  Instead, Fly From Here is caught in between--not quite progressive and not quite radio rock.  There are highlights that include superior-as-usual bass playing and backing vocals from Chris Squire.   But, what is clear about that this incarnation of YES is that it lacks the virtuoso playing and songwriting of Rick Wakeman, and without the \\"lead\\" keyboards, Steve Howe and Geoff Downes need to step up and write and play melodies that provide more than ethereal accompaniment to soft-rock sounding Benoit vocals.  The lack of classic YES-sounding guitar and keyboard parts, and the lack of classic odd-time beats, exposes Alan White, who is a great rock drummer, but not great enough to add any extra elements that Bill Bruford might have.   YES needs to figure out what type of band they want to be.  If they want to be a rock band, they need to write catchy tunes that radio listeners can sing along to.  If they want to return to their progressive roots, theyneed to commit wholeheartedly to writing the difficult to play, yet inspirational epics that set the standard for the next generation of progressive metal bands like Dream Theater.   Something in between, which is what Fly From Here is, falls short of both potential goals.

Thankfully, with the release of, \\"Captain America:  The First Avenger,\\" that string remains unbroken.    \\"Captain America:  The First Avenger,\\" tells the story of Steve Rogers, a scrawny weakling desperate to get into World War Two, despite his tiny form and long list of health problems.  Feeling left out when his friends and his country head off to war, he's offered the chance to join a program that may help him become a powerful super soldier, and help change the course of the war.  Jumping at the task, he quickly finds himself confronting what may be the Nazi's most powerful figure, and weapons that could end the war, and not in favor of the allies.    What makes \\"Captain America\\" so great is that it knows exactly what it sets out to be:  A homage to the serials of the 30's and 40's, in the vein of Raiders of the Lost Ark (there's even a clever nod to it early on).  Combining elements of war films, science fiction, and pulp fiction, \\"Captain America\\" is a near perfect blend of all three, with great action scenes, an awesome retro feel, and a nice sense of humor.  There's nary a weak note to be found here, as the cast is strong all throughout, with Chris Evans portraying a hero who has no ego, and only wants to do the right thing, and Hugo Weaving proving once more that he can play bad guys like no one else, embodying the scientific madness of the Red Skull.  To top it off, the visuals and production values are top notch throughout, and the film really does feel like a comic book come to life, with each new action sequence outdoing the last.     If I had to nitpick, the only real flaw in the film isa a romance subplot between Steve Rogers and Peggy Carter that I felt was unnecessary.  But even then, the film seems to realize the same thing, as it doesn't go very far, a fact that I'm glad for.  \\"Captain America\\" focuses on constantly providing thrills and adventure, and succeeds wonderfully.  While it does feel a bit long, I didn't mind in the slightest.  If anything, I wanted to see even more of Captain America's world, as this is a film filled with optimism, where the good guys are good, the bad guys are bad, and there's no grim and grittiness that so often drenches today's heroes.      I could keep raving about the action, the costumes, the humor, but I'll leave it at this:  \\"Captain America\\" is now one of my favorite superhero films.  It knows what it wants to do, doesn't deviate from the path, brings all of it's elements together, and provides a light hearted escapism that the world so desperately needs.  If Marvel can keep making movies this entertaining, I can't wait to see what they have down the road!

Loosely adapted from Washington Irving's story, this Tim Burton classic is part romance, part detective story, and part gothic horror -- and Johnny Depp helps elevate it to a brilliant supernatural thriller.<br /><br />Constable Ichabod Crane (Johnny Depp) is sent to the tiny town of Sleepy Hollow to investigate a series of murders. He soon learns that the locals believe that a monstrous undead Hessian known as the Headless Horseman is the one who's beheading people. As a rational man of science, Ichabod thinks this is ridiculous... until he has a close encounter with the Horseman after he committed some of his murders.<br /><br />But Ichabod is still convinced that someone living is controlling the Horseman, and it's all wrapped up in a complex scheme involving a pregnancy, a marriage, a will, and a tangled family tree. But as deaths stack up and heads roll, Ichabod and his orphaned servant Masbeth (Marc Pickering) discover that they are up against evil that they couldn't have even imagined.<br /><br />\\"Sleepy Hollow\\" is vintage Tim Burton, back when he was a little more restrained about said style -- everything is painted in blacks, whites and greys with brief splashes of color (BLOOD!), eerie gothic buildings, and skeletal trees. And there's that little edge of rickety weirdness, especially when Depp whips out his buggy-eyed binoculars.<br /><br />Now, it's not perfect -- the witch's hentai-tongue/eyeballs always make me burst out laughing. But Burton really infuses this movie with a sense of creeping, bloody horror that gets more and more nightmarish, peppered with moments of bleak humor (Ichabod trying to throttle the Horseman) and some really disturbing beheadings. It's a pretty sharp contrast to the rather sweet, shy romance between Ichabod and his host's daughter, Katrina Van Tassel.<br /><br />As for Depp, he's absolutely perfect -- he plays Ichabod Crane as a young man with a horrifying past (which is revealed via dreams) and an abiding love for logic, science and reason. He's also pretty nervy, fragile and neurotic; how often do you see movie heroes who faint more often than the heroines?<br /><br />Christina Ricci is rather wooden at first, but she improves a lot as we discover some of the secrets she keeps hidden. Pickering is excellent as a young orphan who latches onto Ichabod as a surrogate father, and Miranda Richardson is all spidery creepiness as Lady Van Tassel. Also keep an eye out for Christopher Lee, Michael Gambon and Christopher Walken's hilariously over-the-top Horseman.<br /><br />\\"Sleepy Hollow\\" is all creepy atmosphere and weirdness, but it also has a solid story at its center. Blood, magic and a deranged undead horseman, all done with Tim Burton's gothic style.n with a horrifying past (which is revealed via dreams) and an abiding love for logic, science and reason. B001RCD2DW|5.0|These headphones are truly amazing! When I was considering them I've read quite a bit about (1) how they are not worth the money without a decent headphone amp, (2) that their bass leaves a lot to desire, and (3) that they have an insanely long burn-in period. All of these criticisms are greatly exaggerated and obscure how wonderfully accurate and detailed these headphones really are.     On the first point, although plugging them into my iPod (as expected) did not produce spectacular results, they performed just fine and definitely better than what many reviews would have you believe. I have a pair of Shure's SE 535, and although I did not do a side by side  A to B comparison (the Shures broke in less than a month of use and I sent them back to the company for service), I do not recall a striking difference in the sound quality between the two when playing out of the iPod. Although, for instance, the iPod driven Shures had somewhat better bass, the AKGs make up for it in other respects (i.e. sound stage). However, it was when I plugged the AKGs into my Onkyo (not super fancy, mid-class) receiver and listened to CDs and a records, they absolutely sung! The sound stage is nice and wide. You can really pinpoint the location of every sound in 3D space. When listening to vocals, it actually feels like you can reach out and touch the singer. Every sound is crisp, precise and easily distinguished from all others. For instance, you can pick out the separate layers of vocal harmonies, that can often be muddled and fused will lesser audio equipment. I should also add that when visiting my parents, I plugged these cans into my parent's Sony \\"space-age\\" looking, CD and tape (!) playing stereo, which was bought at Target or Walmart in late 1990's/early 2000's, is now relegated to the basement. When playing a CD, considering the source, the headphones blew me away! Although I don't doubt that a headphone amp would be nice, it is not absolutely necessary to realize the many benefits of these headphones.       On the second point, regarding the bass, I could not disagree more. Unless you like your bass so heavy that it muddles everything else, these headphones are for you. The bass is controlled, punchy and tight, and does not muddle the other frequencies. It is very natural. I listen to tons of electronic music (electronica, glitch, techno, minimal, house-- you name it -- all of which is considered bass driven), and I think these headphones are perfect. In fact they give you a perfect balance of bass, nicely transparent mids and crisp highs. I've listened to Senns HD 650 (which I do like) but actually prefer these, due to that clarity. BTW, other types of music sound great as well with these headphones. Besides electronic, I also listen to classical music, opera, rock, and jazz, and I think that they sound great with all. For a while, these cans will definitely be my go to headphones for everything I listen to.     Lastly, although it is widely publicized that these cans have a very long burn-in period, I was actually impressed with the out-of-the-box sound. I did not do a dedicated burn-in as some do (leaving them to play 24/7 somewhere in a closet). Rather, I just kept on having dedicated listening session every few days, and noticed them opening up right in front of my eyes. I love them now, but I am really looking forward to hearing what they sound like in few more months!     Overall, if you are dissuaded from getting these cans due to issues some have called out online, take those criticisms with a grain of salt. If you like detailed and precise reproduction of what your CDs, records and digital files really sound like, try these cans. I don't think you'll be disappointed.

B000001FE0|5.0|This album shows what truly made the Bee Gees great and that they stood on their own merit of great songs and wonderful productions. This album is their Crowning Achievement of the 4 they would release in the 1960's in only a short 2 year period! This remaster was done wonderfully and even has the original red velvet cover with the big gold lettering of the original. As an earlier reviewer stated, this album definitely got the royal treatment! From a historical perspective, Odessa was such a strong statement by the band and was one of three epic double albums released that year by a British Band, the other 2 being \\"Tommy\\" by the Who and \\"White\\" album as it's called by the Beatles.<br /><br />Although never really thought of in this way, the Bee Gees were a part of the last wave of the \\"British Invasion\\" bands and were, even at their young age, contemporaries of the other bands such as the Beatles, Rolling Stones, Who, Yardbirds, etc. This album was the one that there manager, Robert Stigwood, really wanted to be a kind of \\"statement\\" album. The band was growing up and their songs and sound, already incredible, were becoming more mature and evolving. The comparisions to the Beatles have always been uncalled for in my opinion as I have always felt that the brothers had a sound and style of writing that was uniquely theirs.<br /><br />Barry & Robin Gibb, in my opinion, were every bit the writers as Lennon/McCartney or Townshend/Entwhistle or Jaggar/Richards and this album is proof of how intricate and diverse their songs could be. The epic title track shows their strong ability to \\"story tell\\" as they take us on a journey across the atlantic ocean. Then you have songs with a country fell such as \\"Marley Purt Drive\\" to the folk sounding \\"Melody Fair\\" to the bouncy \\"Suddenly\\" amongst others. Maurice Gibbs wonderful talent for writing melodies and being able to play multiple instruments really comes through strong as well.<br /><br />This album really showed that the \\"boys\\" were growing up musically, at the time of its release, Barry was 22 and Robin and Maurice 19. Unfortunately, after the release of this album, problems arose to the point that Robin left the band and the next album was recorded without him. Fortunately for us, their fans, he would return after a brief hiatus and they would then begin the second phase of their incredible careers. That being said, Odessa was truly the Crown Jewel of the early phase of their careers and a wonderful album, fresh and clean, all these years later.s\\" were growing up musically, at the time of its release, Barry was 22 and Robin and Maurice 19. B0025KVLT2|5.0|My love for this CD is second to none, yet I have a problem with those reviewers who contend that this is the group's first masterpiece and that those CDs released before it were examples of \\"bubble gum pop\\".  I would argue that \\"A Hard Day's Night\\" is a towering piece of art which makes no concessions to anyone or anything.  Far from being \\"bubble gum\\" it boasts an astonishing number of styles and of great, memorable tracks: the only weak cut may be \\"When I Get Home,\\" the remainder are works of genius which must not be subsumed as unimportant or as trivial beneath the weight of the later, more experimental CDs. 1461190460|4.0|Interesting book, odd ideas.  I never read this growing up.  I read it only a few years ago.  As a philosopher, I was quite interested by several chapters (10-15) in which the prince encounters oddball characters on small planets by themselves.  There is a subtle philosophical humor in these encounters because the characters engage in actions or have thoughts that are not odd in themselves, but are distinctly odd in the context in which they live, because the thoughts or actions depend for their sense on a context quite different from the one they exist in.  The scenarios make for reflection by the reader on questions like what does it take for something to be an \\"order\\" when there is no convention of obedience?  a case of \\"admiration\\" when there is nothing to compare with? what does it take to \\"own\\" something beyond simply claiming it?  can habits continue to make sense when speeded up from once a day to once a minute?  can geography make sense without exploration?  I know these are not the parts of the book that appeal to most readers, but they are illustrative of points that Wittgenstein tries to make in his Philosophical Investigations--namely that we misunderstand things when we do not appreciate the importance of their context.  This little book was published in 1944, well after Wittgenstein was working on his investigations, but before they were published in 1953.  So there is no likelihood of interaction between the two men.  Likely their thoughts are completely independent.  But it is interesting to me that they approach similar issues in quite different ways.

0553386794|5.0|A Game of Thrones invites comparisons to J.R.R. Tolkien's The Lord of the Rings and Robert Jordan's The Wheel of Time.  The series rivals those in quality but is very different in style and tone.  The Lord of the Rings and The Wheel of Time are about epic battles between good and evil; A Game of Thrones is similarly epic in scope, but the good and evil are contained within each character.  It is, at its heart, about power and its acquisition.  The tone is far darker than the aforementioned series and is unrelenting.<br /><br />A Game of Thrones opens (after a prologue revealing frozen undead in the far north) with Lord Eddard Stark being asked by his boyhood friend, King Robert Baratheon, to ride south to serve as the King's right-hand man (literally, his official title is Hand of the King).  The promotion, along with suspicions around an accident in Eddard's family and the circumstances of the last Hand's death, sets off a high stakes feud between the Starks and the powerful Lannister family.  Meanwhile, across the Narrow Sea, the rightful heir to the throne (his father, the last Targaryen king, was usurped by Robert in a rebellion) and his sister set a plot into motion to take back the throne.<br /><br />Martin tells his story in through the familiar third person limited narrative structure, cycling between eight different POV characters (not including the prologue).  Outside of Tyrion, the black sheep of House Lannister, and Princess Daenerys, of House Targaryen, the POV characters are all members of the Stark family.  Martin takes a more subjective approach than, say, Jordan in The Wheel of Time, making limited use of the unreliable narrator for dramatic purposes.<br /><br />What unfolds is an engrossing tale of family, power, and intrigue in a world where the events of a rebellion twenty years prior are as relevant as the events of yesterday.  Where other fantasy epics focus on battles and swordfights, the conflict at the center of A Game of Thrones centers around politics.  A dark secret begins to unfold and loyalties are uncertain.<br /><br />Martin has been much lauded for his gritty, realistic depiction of medieval life, and he never lets us forget that his is a Hobbesian world, where life is nasty, brutish, and short. Martin also has a talent for zigging where the reader expects him to zag, and the plot begins hurtling toward the shocking climax little more than halfway through the book.<br /><br />A Game of Thrones is the first volume of the A Song of Ice and Fire series, now at five of a projected seven books. I entered the Yes world at age 11 in 1984, buying a cassette of \\"90125\\" after liking \\"Owner Of A Lonely Heart\\", \\"Leave It\\" and \\"It Can Happen\\"--all Trevor Rabin penned songs. This might cause anger and disgust with older Yes fans as I am sure many of them did not/do not enjoy the Rabin-era Yes (1983-1994). I sure do, but I also liked \\"Anderson, Bruford, Wakeman, Howe\\". I think it's fair to say that most 11 year old kids were not listening to Yes, even if they were more marketable in the 1980's. Since that time I have embraced progressive rock: King Crimson, ELP, Genesis, Rush, but Yes win the title of best progressive rock band of all time in my book. I now own all of their albums, and I listened to all of them in sequential order leading up to \\"Fly From Here\\".<br /><br />My first listen was jarring. Benoit David's vocals are not Jon Anderson. Trevor Horn, Chris Squire and Steve Howe cleverly mimicked Jon Anderson on 1980's \\"Drama\\", but the same is not true here. Benoit David is serviceable, but he does not have the range or depth of Jon Anderson's one-of-a-kind vocals. He also is not a primary songwriter here. This means he is singing what the band has given him. That must have been difficult. It is much harder to sing something that you did not write, and you might not feel committed to. Still, with repeated listening, Benoit David's vocals end up being fine, but in no way does it change my preference for Jon Anderson whether he has asthma or other respiratory issues or not. Whether his voice isn't the same now that he is in his late 60's or not. Jon Anderson is Jon Anderson, founding member of Yes.<br /><br />I'm tired of all the talk about Trevor Horn and Geoffrey Downes resurrecting old Buggles tracks for this album. I don't care. I liked The Buggles, and I like Yes. The problem I have with the suite of \\"Fly From Here\\" that opens the album is why did the band feel they needed to make six separate parts? There was a time when Yes would boldly make 20+ minute songs, heck, they'd even make a double album of four long tracks. What happened here? Personally, I think they should have ditched the overture, \\"Bumpy Ride\\" and the reprise and just stuck with the three main tracks: \\"We Can Fly\\", \\"Sad Night At The Airfield\\" and \\"Madman At The Screens\\". These three songs combined, without any stupid segues, would have made a great singular opening track--re-asserting that Yes is back with a vengeance. But no, they divide the song up into six parts that lose focus.<br /><br />The second half of \\"Fly From Here\\" is a total mess. Chris Squire's \\"The Man You Always Wanted Me To Be\\" is one of the weakest Yes songs ever made and reeks of vanity project. \\"Life On A Film Set\\" is okay, but nothing special. Surprisingly, it is Steve Howe who saves the album's second half with \\"Hour Of Need\\" which sounds like a classic Yes ballad, not unlike something Jon Anderson might come up with. Then there's Howe's instrumental \\"Solitaire\\". Much has been said about this track as being one of his finest ever. Really? I dare say that I like \\"Sketches In The Sun\\" from Howe's side project \\"GTR\\" from 1986 (I actually own it on cd, and I bought it on cassette originally in May 1986 when it was released at the tender age of 13). Lastly, \\"Into The Storm\\" finishes off the album pretty nicely. I have to admit that I didn't really notice Oliver Wakeman's input on the keyboards. I'm sure he's there, but his playing blends seamlessly like a session musician's rather than standing out. I mean, Rick Wakeman's contributions stood out. I knew he was playing. Not so for Oliver, and that may be no fault of his own, but more Horn and Squire's doing.<br /><br />As other reviewers have said here, this isn't Yes' best work or worst. The guys are old now, not just older. They chose to not have Jon Anderson this time out. It's been a decade between albums (that's several generations of music listeners who are just now finding out about Yesfor the first time). \\"Fly From Here\\" is passable, and does seem to get better with repeated listening. So three stars for being above average Yes.<br /><br />Here's how \\"Fly From Here\\" compares with Yes' previous works:<br /><br />1969 Yes: Three and a Half Stars<br />1970 Time And A Word: Three and a Half Stars<br />1971 The Yes Album: Four Stars<br />1971 Fragile: Five Stars<br />1972 Close To The Edge: Five Stars<br />1973 Tales From Topographic Oceans: Three and a Half Stars<br />1974 Relayer: Four Stars<br />1977 Going For The One: Three and a Half Stars<br />1978 Tormato: Four Stars<br />1980 Drama: Three and a Half Stars<br />1983 90125: Five Stars<br />1987 Big Generator: Three and a Half Stars<br />1989 Anderson, Bruford, Wakeman, Howe: Five Stars<br />1991 Union: Three Stars<br />1994 Talk: Four Stars<br />1996-1997 Keystudio: Four Stars<br />1997 Open Your Eyes: Two and a Half Stars<br />1999 The Ladder: Three and a Half Stars<br />2001 Magnification: Four Stars<br />2011 Fly From Here: Three Starsere\\" is passable, and does seem to get better with repeated listening. So three stars for being above average Yes.    Here's how \\"Fly From Here\\" compares with Yes' previous works:    1969 Yes: Three and a Half Stars  1970 Time And A Word: Three and a Half Stars  1971 The Yes Album: Four Stars  1971 Fragile: Five Stars  1972 Close To The Edge: Five Stars  1973 Tales From Topographic Oceans: Three and a Half Stars  1974 Relayer: Four Stars  1977 Going For The One: Three and a Half Stars  1978 Tormato: Four Stars  1980 Drama: Three and a Half Stars  1983 90125: Five Stars  1987 Big Generator: Three and a Half Stars  1989 Anderson, Bruford, Wakeman, Howe: Five Stars  1991 Union: Three Stars  1994 Talk: Four Stars  1996-1997 Keystudio: Four Stars  1997 Open Your Eyes: Two and a Half Stars  1999 The Ladder: Three and a Half Stars  2001 Magnification: Four Stars  2011 Fly From Here: Three Stars

And on the night before the climactic battle, two of them know the next day their units will be facing each other in battle, and are agonizing over it. Even after seeing it 6-7 times, I still cannot help but weep when one of the friends cries out to God for the fate of this trio near the end of the movie.    As a veteran, when a brave officer whose unit is out of ammunition orders a desperate charge, I shudder uncontrollably, and my only thought is \\"He did his duty.\\"  I am also fascinated by the variety of reasons the soldiers on both sides had for fighting in the war, which the movie wonderfully articulates.    As a citizen, I am very conflicted.  I am thankful that the union was preserved and slavery abolished.  But I shudder at how states rights were weakened; indeed, for decades now in my opinion the Tenth Amendment has been virtually ignored.    As a Christian, I find it fascinating and thought-provoking  how more overtly Christian officers and soldiers on both sides were, and am intrigued by Col. Chamberlain, a devout Christian.    As a student of culture and society, I am fascinated at the differences between southern and northern cultures and societies which are clearly depicted throughout the movie. 0743277465|4.0|Josh Waitzkin is an over achiever. He began competing in chess tournaments around the age of eight and was consistently the highest rated player for his age group until he stopped competing in his late `teens for personal reasons. Starting in 1998, he mastered Tai Chi, winning a pair of world championship titles a few years later. He now runs the JW Foundation, a nonprofit helping students direct their own learning in a way that meshes with who they are as people.    The Art of Learning is as multifaceted as its author: a compelling autobiography, a discussion of the theory and practice of both chess and Tai Chi, an exposition on general and performance psychology, and a primer on philosophy. I have distilled the most pertinent lessons for the UnCollege community:    Everything is Interconnected    The deepest message conveyed in the book is that many seemingly disparate pursuits are actually connected in subtle ways. A perceptive person can learn much about martial arts from chess, such as, and vice versa. In a earlier post, I sought to break down the barrier between \\"common sense\\" and \\"book smarts\\", and that sentiment is echoed here in a slightly revised form. Josh writes:    \\"From the outside Tai Chi and chess couldn't be more different, but they began to converge in my mind. I started to translate my chess ideas into Tai Chi language, as if the two arts were linked by an essential connecting ground. Every day I noticed more and more similarities, until I began to feel as if I were studying chess when I was studying Tai Chi\\" (xvi).\\"    The first step is to learn to think of our various pursuits not as disconnected islands, but rather as part of a whole, each part informing the others. As you go about your day, actively look for ways to learn x from y.    Master the Fundamentals    Although this is hardly an earth-shattering idea, Josh manages to give it unique expression, illustrating the urgency of practicing the basics with examples from his own life. He credits his successes in the early parts of his martial arts career not to having a broad mastery of many tactics, but with having a deep mastery of just a few. The mechanics behind one a single technique are often the same ones that lie behind the entire system. A big part of this mastery consists in gradually internalizing the fundamental principles until they are unconscious and reflexive. This concept is referred to in the book alternatively as numbers to leave numbers and form to leave form, and it applies to every pursuit.    Josh compares modern people to fish swimming at the surface, so caught up with the ripples and currents there that they miss the beauty of the abyss below. While there is much truth to this, there is still something to be said for generalization, omnivorous consumption of skills, and polymathy. We should combine both approaches: depth and breadth. Sometimes all we want, need, or have time to obtain is a broad perspective on a field or discipline. That's okay, because every bit is going to stretch our minds and enrich our perspectives. However, if all we ever do is skim the surface, then we're missing out on the value of plunging deeply and intimately into an art. Likewise, cultivating long-term tunnel vision on a single domain of knowledge will mean losing opportunities to see connections between disparate activities and the myriad ways in which one practice informs another.    Invest in Failure    One of the more difficult parts of the learning process is bouncing back from the inevitable setbacks and failures. Those of us with a self-image to protect may find shelving our egos and giving ourselves permission to lose particularly trying. By staying focused on the goal - improving as people, not proving how great we already are - we can continue learning. Confidence and pride in our accomplishments have their place, but should never get in the way of personal development.    Learn to Love the Journey    As important as gaining mastery of a skill is, cultivating a love for the process of learning is just as important. If we make the reasonable assumption that even masters still have aspects on which they can improve, it stands to reason that we all spend a lot more time as learners than we probably realize. This principle is so important, Josh places it squarely at the center of success in any field, saying that the path to the top lies \\"in a well-though-out approach that inspires resilience, the ability to make connections between diverse pursuits, and day-to-day enjoyment of the process.\\" (30)    Learning with Style    An overarching theme of the book, and one of the main tenets espoused by Josh's nonprofit, is to make every aspect of our learning harmonious with and an extension of our personality. Great performers of all stripes know who they are and how to synchronize their activities with their mindsets. In chess, Josh writes, mistakes on the board often expose psychological weaknesses. The reverse is also true; knowing how a person acts under stress or pressure can tell you a lot about how they will play chess. As he began competing at a higher and higher level, Josh kept notes on how he felt during tournaments, specifically when he made moves that turned out later to be watersheds. This practice, continued later with his Tai Chi training, allowed him to grow more and more attuned with himself as a performer. He was able to quickly spot lapses in concentration and repair them, thus strengthening his technique from the inside out. I have kept a journal for years for this reason, and recommend that you all do the same. As you learn more about yourself through writing or introspection, try to find ways to make your pursuits an expression of yourself. The way you play chess, cook a meal, or write a book review should reflect a deepening awareness of your strengths and dispositions.      Read the original review here:  [...]

0553801473|2.0|I started these books back in 1998 when I was young and still enjoyed the fantasy genre, and it is one of the few series I kept up with as new books came out. I still think he has a chance of doing something special since he doesn't follow the stock rules that so many other writers of fantasy do, but he is hopelessly mired down in his own work now.<br /><br />He has two huge problems with this series, and they are never more front and center then they are with 'Dance Of Dragons'.  He has no idea what the end of his own story is and he has no desire to write a self-contained book.<br /><br />  Since 'Feast of Crows' and 'Dance With Dragons' were published as separate works covering the same time frame, you would have thought he would have been able to take great strides with the plot, but it is sadly not the case.  In earlier works, he would skip events both trivial and monumental and recap them a in one view point.  Now he describes every event in every view point, making for an uneventful and unsatisfying read-does he realize how many chapters he has describing what Master Illyrio eats while having none about the Whispering Woods?  Most of this book is in adding some details to back stories, introducing some more characters and gearing up for the next work.  I can only imagine these are stall tactic because he has no idea where to take the series.<br /><br />  Perhaps the most unsatisfying feature of dance is the five hundred cliff hangers he felt compelled to leave us with.  Try this simple formula next time: exposition, confrontation, conclusion.  Even if you are writing a series, you can't put out a 1000 page novel of exposition and expect to please anyone. They will be even more displeased when you leave every thread of your plot in a crucial moment.<br /><br />  On a personal note, there are touches he added to this book that I find annoying.  Back is the acrobatic Tyrion after a one sentence appearance in Game of Thrones.  A hesitance to kill off his characters has emerged along with a propensity for bringing them back.  Also, he appears to back step from some of the events he has foreshadowed throughout the series, almost as if he was annoyed that people had guessed them to easily.  Whether these are just red herons he threw in along with everything else or precursors to getting rid of the mythology he had hinted at is hard to tell.  But I was far far from pleased at many of the twists he does add to 'Dance With Dragons'.<br /><br /> The bottom line is 'Dance With Dragons' needed more time with an editor. The short window between completion and publication made this book suffer, and if he doesn't have an editor that is willing to throw out some of his chapters, he needs to find one willing to stand up to him.  He will be better off for it. B001HN690O|4.0|I guess I will have to make a confession that will get the Dreamworks crowd all worked up: while I enjoyed Kung Fu Panda, I'm not one of the many who hold it up on as high a pedestal as the superior Monsters Vs. Aliens or the movie that may be the epitome of all things cute, How to Train Your Dragon. So nail me to the cross if you want. I also have, in recent times, developed a distaste for sequels. This year we had Pixar's first terrible movie (Cars 2), the disastrously messy Pirates 4, and the carbon copy Hangover 2 (though Deathly Hallows Part 2 is my pick for top movie of 2011)<br /><br />So while I, as I stated, enjoyed KFP1, I initially had low expectations for it's sequel in place. And with that in mind, I can say I enjoyed Kung Fu Panda 2. Although it's not perfect, it managed to be very entertaining, and a worthy follow up.<br /><br />The sequel takes place some time after the first movie. Po has continued training to be a true Kung Fu master, but there's one thing he hasn't yet truly mastered: inner peace. Of course, to a panda like Po, Kung Fu is all about kicking butt, but he soon learns that the only way to be a true Kung Fu master is to be at peace with himself. However, a new gang, sent in by a Peacock who seems a ghost of Po's past, has terrorized the village, sending Po and the gang on an adventure to stop him.<br /><br />Kung Fu Panda 2 works... for the most part. The good news is, it overall provides for an entertaining ride that the whole family can enjoy. The script is filled with tons of funny lines that are bound to have the kids repeating them for hours on end. As well as the voice cast is just as charming this time around; Jack Black still manages to charm as Po, Angelina Jolie still manages to put her street tough kick to good use. However, Gary Oldman, as usual, steals the show. He makes a great villain andit shows.<br /><br />The animation also manages to dazzle. I can safely say that the animation quality has come a long way since Shrek. I didn't see this in 3D, but I guarantee I would have loved to. There are a lot of moments in the animation where I wanted to reach out and run my fingers through Po's fur, or move my hand along the cannon near the end, and had I seen this movie in 3D, I probably would have reached out and tried to feel them! It reminded me of when I saw Sanctum in 3D earlier this year, where I felt like I was actually underwater.<br /><br />However there are a few flaws that prevent me from calling it a perfect film, let alone near perfect. For starters, it took me a while to be able to connect with the movie, as the pacing unfortunately is rushed to the point where it moves at a machine gun pace. Thepacing does slighty slow down in the middle but even then, the film still feels like the filmmakers were trying to cram too much in. Also, the story seemed convoluted. There was a story but it was rushed, to the point where I had myself questioning halfway through the film what the movie was about.<br /><br />Overall, Kung Fu Panda 2 is a very enjoyable film that is not without it's flaws. I would say go into it with an open mind, and don't expect Pixar level entertainment. Oh, and even if you don't go to movies for the actors, it's still worth seeing for Gary Oldman alone.<br /><br />Overall: 7/10<br /><br />Pros: The film provides good action, humour, laughs, alongside a stellar voice cast, a terrific Hans Zimmer score and beautiful animation that would convince even 3D skeptics to buy a ticket.<br /><br />Cons: The film's pacing is rushed, to the near point of being disorienting, as well as the story could have been more fleshed out as well as the villain.I guarantee I would have loved to. B0042RJWTC|5.0|First and foremost, this series is a docudrama, not a documentary, and it does not pretend to be a documentary. Thank goodness is doesn't. Showtime's take on the Tudor dynasty is in the same vein as \\"Deadwood\\", another very highly rated series produced by HBO. Other than Jonathan Rhys Meyer not putting on about 250 lbs or donning increasing larger body suits and makeup as the series progressed, it followed the true history of his reign and of the times pretty well as far as the major plots and players go.     The \\"history\\", such as it is, that could and should be taken from this series is 1) Henry VIII was a brilliant, narcissistic, charismatic leader who, 2) wanted a male heir and loved the fairer sex so much that he divorced (literally and figuratively) England from the Roman Catholic church for the former, and went through six wives in pursuit of the latter and the former. Much of the series revolves around the excesses of court, especially Henry's. Sets, dialogue, and wardrobe are \\"gussied-up\\" for the show, no doubt, but it would be less enjoyable to watch if they tried to stay period perfect to the early 1500s. Since there are obviously no photos from the time, everything must be interpreted from literature, art, and artifacts. As still happens today, the literature and art of the time most certainly took certain license with the \\"truth\\", and was even seen as business as usual.    The acting is first rate, the cinematography is beautiful (and disgusting where it needs to be), and the story well-told. Some have commented on a preoccupation with sex and nudity, and Showtime could certainly have left it to audience imagination, showing a litany of characters walking into bed chambers, and then cutting to sunrise next morning. But seriously, this is not 1961, with Rob and Laura Petrie sleeping in separate beds. The King alone had 6 wives, and countless mistresses (as did many Popes and priests of that era), and procreation to get heirs was a driving force behind many decisions and the politics of the upper classes of that time. That's where little Tudors come from, after all.      This is a very entertaining and engaging production, and must be viewed as such. Best to leave a more prosaic, accurate and up-to-date treatment of the history to PBS, which would be most welcome as well.

B0002YJ29O|4.0|With name performers cancelling, the Verbier Festival seems to be getting some negative notice these days, so I thought I'd share my review here as a reminder of what it used to be.<br /><br />This DVD evidently just got kerplopped on the market with zilch publicity, around the time that saw the release of \\"The 5 Browns,\\" another thingamabob of multipiano performances. I was afraid it would get lost in the shuffle, and apparently it did.<br /><br />But it does have its considerable virtues. To get an idea of the level of musicianship involved, take a look at the \\"cast list\\":<br /><br />Pianists: Martha Argerich, Evgeny Kissin, Emanuel Ax, Leif Ove Andsnes, Mikhail Pletnev, Claude Frank, Staffan Scheja (remember his one RCA Victor LP?), Lang Lang, Nicolas Angelich and James Levine. It is to gasp.<br /><br />The string players are no less impressive: Gidon Kremer, Vadim Repin, Renaud Capuon, Sarah Chang, Ilya Gringolts, Yuri Bashmet, Nobuko Imai, Christian Tetzlaff, Nikolaj Znaider, Dmitry Sitkovetsky, Mischa Maisky, Boris Pergamenschikow, and a double-bassist I haven't heard of before, Patrick de los Santos. Fans of the delightful Barbara Hendricks will want to buy this for her cameo performance of \\"Happy Birthday\\" (with royalties properly paid to Warner/Chappell, I imagine).<br /><br />The reason I grabbed this is not the 38 minutes' worth of Martha, although I'm sure her admirers will want it for that reason. It's the presence of the one-movement Smetana sonata, of which I used to possess a score back in my undegraduate days. (Chris O'Riley also featured this on an episode of \\"From the Top\\" a few weeks ago.) Gottschalk's \\"L'Union,\\" in an eight-piano edition, also makes an appearance here.<br /><br />The Good: This DVD has performances by nine major pianist-musicians, plus Lang Lang, and a string section to die for. It has actual music, complete works yet, by Bach, Mozart, Smetana, and Gottschalk. None of your pop fluff here! And if this sort of thing is important to you, it has four bona-fide musical babes, namely Argerich, Chang, Imai, and Hendricks.<br /><br />The Not-So-Good: These first-rate musicians all appear to be over 25, so they lose the \\"cuteness\\" factor contest, in this mad world where youth often seems to be prized well over experience. Barbara Hendricks, though still adorable, is no longer quite the lovely wisp I met twenty-five years ago. And the lighting appears to be blackness with spotlight on the performers, which gets fatiguing after a while. And some of the items (Wagner \\"Walk&uuml;renritt,\\" R-K \\"Fumblebee,\\" etc.) are a mess instead of music.<br /><br />Still, I loved hearing Kissin and Argerich in the Mozart K. 521, definitely the highlight of the whole concert; it's almost too bad in a way that it was placed first, because none of the rest of the program comes quite up to that level. The JS Bach concerto (for four keyboards after Vivaldi) is solidly, if non-HIPly, done.<br /><br />I have plenty of catty observations. The first is that any rock- or pop-aficionado who thinks that classical musicians all look alike, or perform alike, should see this DVD, because they don't, and they were all still having a grand old time. Naturally, since they all consented to be seen, I'd make just a few little recommendations:<br /><br />Kissin should either let that 'stache grow out, or get it waxed already. Here it looks like a mistake (and it probably is). As for his 'fro, well, Levine has a bigger one, but Maisky has them both beat, no contest! (But what is that thing on his necklace, an IDIC he got from Mr. Spock?) If I had a choice for a night of passion with Jessica Simpson, Britney Spears, Jennifer Lopez, or Martha Argerich, of course I'd still pick jowly little Martha (though with a long regretful look at Lopez' behind), although she might want to get those roots touched up a bit.<br /><br />Sarah Chang looks as though she's always nearly at the point of laughing out loud, but of course that's not a bad thing.Which brings me to the four gloomy Northerners, namely Kissin (again!), Pletnev, Schaja and Andsnes. CHEER UP, guys! Move to Southern California, already; it worked wonders for Esa-Pekka!<br /><br />Lang Lang. Sigh. Where to begin? Perhaps he should take deportment lessons from Fou Ts'ong, so he can learn to project the music, rather than draw attention to himself. Maybe he should find out which monastery Peter Maag lived in, and hole up there to meditate for a couple of years. Maybe his doctor could prescribe iodine pills to do something about those bulging eyes (well, it worked for my brother). If he insists on carrying on this way, he should be sent to Sarasota, Florida - oh, wait, they closed the Clown College back in 1997, didn't they? Well, there are times when I wonder when he will simply abandon all pretense and give recitals wearing a rhinestone-and-sequin suit, and with a candelabra atop his piano.<br /><br />But my most lasting impression is much more easily stated. We need more musicians like Claude Frank, and fewer moppets as make-believe opera singers.<br /><br />Recommended with reservations, but you'll know if you want it.Andsnes. CHEER UP, guys! 0805209999|5.0|I read \\"The Trial\\" when I was in my early teens and I still haven't found a book that I have found more significant. I have tried reading authors considered in the same vein as Kafka, like Walser (beautifully crafted prose, but lacking purpose) or Pelevin (entertaining, but gimmicky), and none are a match. Even within Kafka's scattered body of work this is the best he produced.<br /><br />I never understood why people peg Kafka as a surrealist. I don't think people who claim this understood what they read. He writes about our bondage to social norms, how we are enslaved by them, and about power structures in human relationships. It's easy to understand this, when we read the \\"letter\\" he wrote to his father, a domineering personality, who had such a devastating influence in Kafka's life. Sure enough, many of the settings in his stories are exaggerated or have a supernatural element to them, but the absurdity of every day life is real and true.<br /><br />There are critics who give Kafka's works a Jewish interpretation, which is fine and probably makes sense, given that Kafka was Jewish. But I'm not Jewish, and when I first read this as an adolescent at the beach (the perfect place to read Kafka), I completely related to preposterous situations which K. -- the story's protagonist -- was faced with, and his inability to react to them, trapped as he was in the \\"must-do's\\" of conventional society. This is completely universal, and that's the hallmark of great works. While he attempts to struggle at first, by the end of the book, K. has been lulled into indifference, surrendering the control of his own life. Charged with some unspecified crime, he allows himself to be driven through a trial over which he has no grasp, and in reality, there is no escaping the sentence. If you examine your life, or perhaps even the lives of those around you, you may reach the unsettling conclusion that this is a true-to-life story about everyone you know.

B00518HBD0|1.0|3 Doors Down is a highly talented band capable of crafting good songs but in dire need of a change in direction.   \\"Time of My Life\\" is a major disappointment from this fan who appreciates their work and has seen this band perform multiple times.    The same two song archetypes are basically repeated ad nauseum.   3 Doors Down are the kings of the slow anthem with the rising chorus.  Examples include \\"Heaven\\", \\"When You're Young\\", \\"Back to Me\\" and the anemic poor choice for lead single \\"What's Left\\".   Problem is, these songs vary from each other so minutely that you could almost intersperse sections from each into a single track without realizing they originated from different parents.  These cannot compete with their older and better cousins, such as \\"Here Without You\\" or \\"Here By Me\\".      The other familiar 3 Doors Down pattern is the unthreatening, mid-tempo rock song such as \\"On the Run\\" and sound-alikes \\"Every Time You Go\\" and \\"Round and Round\\".  \\"Race for the Sun\\" teases you with an all too brief guitar solo, effectively dampened by the droning of the rhythm section. \\"My Way\\" tosses in a dash of vocal effects, but this is like adding a mere ten granules of salt to your mashed potatoes.  Their compositions just fade into the background like the tepid \\"She is Love.\\"      The sole exception is the song \\"Believer\\" which shows that this band can indeed branch out when they try.    I would love to see what a different producer would have done with these tracks.   I disagree with bassist Todd Harrell's pride in being a \\"no frills\\" band.  3 Doors Down, you know how to write a song, but please give your listeners some variation in your sonic landscape.    I'll take anything - a jacked up bass line, a vocal duet, energetic guitar riffs, some rawer vocals, different drumming techniques....hell, even a horn section!  Otherwise you will be denigrated to becoming a \\"no thrills\\" band.     I defy anyone to listen to the full songs or clips end-to-end without glazing over.Such a shame!  This is the band capable of so much more (see \\"Citizen Soldier\\", \\"Duck and Run\\" and \\"Kryptonite\\").  Evolve or fade away.
