0765324849|3.0|*Will Contain  Slight Spoilers*<br /><br />\\"Bioshock Rapture\\" is essentially what the front and back cover dictates: the rise and fall of Rapture. This book was a worthwhile welcome to the game's major success and is pretty much what most fans wanted to read: a prequel rich in background information about the characters in the game and how their decisions and actions brought them to their eventual end. The book not only has this, but it also has 100 pages worth of backstory detailing how Rapture was built. With the book being 430 pages, there could be room for a decent story.<br /><br />The problem with \\"Rapture\\" comes in the insane amount of POVs, which damage the overall impact that some of these characters have on the story as well as the impact they might've had on the readers. These are the POVs, and are not in any chronological order:<br /><br />Sullivan<br />Andrew Ryan<br />Bill McDonagh<br />Frank Fontaine<br />Stanley Poole<br />Tenenbaum<br />Sofia Lamb<br />Steinman<br />Rupert Mudge<br />Sam Lutz<br />Simon Wales<br />Martin Finnegan<br />Diane McClintock<br />Elaine McDonagh<br /><br />That is a lot of characters, with Bill McDonagh taking up more than half the POVs in the entire book. Most of the characters, like Sam Lutz or Elaine McDonagh, have only one POV, while others have POVs so insignificant that they were much better off removed from the story entirely. It all boils down to personal tastes when it comes to these POVs. The problem is that they are barely developed, with exception of Bill, who is the only character with decent amount of character development. It probably has to do with the fact that Bill has 36 segments told through his perspective.<br /><br />On top of that, the book has twenty chapters with each chapter being split into POV breaks with locations and a year. From 1945 to 1959, this book also has to tie in all of these events together and not once did it have the feeling that such a long amount of time has passed. The characters don't seem to age nor change in their viewpoints and seem rather settled into their roles and stay that way throughout, with the exception of slight changes.<br /><br />Another problem with this book is the convoluted explanations that make certain events sound way too complicated then they have to be. One of the more annoying ones would be how Frank Fontaine discovered that Ryan was building a city at the bottom of the sea. There are tons of undercover cops and detectives trying to figure out what Ryan is doing, yet Fontaine is able to figure it all out by pretending to be a cop and interrogating one of Ryan's crew members. Wouldn't it have been simpler, and perhaps better, if Fontaine was just another smuggler selling supplies to Rapture?<br /><br />There were some rather enjoyable parts in this novel, like the odd relationship between Tenenbaum and Fontaine and the one between Sander Cohen and Martin Finnegan. Too bad they were very quickly brushed aside to make room for other events that weren't of much impact. The ending itself leaves much to be desired, as it felt more like a very forced happy ending then something that naturally happened.<br /><br />In all, I would say it's an average video game novel with some rises that make it a worthwhile read. The characters do feel like the ones in the game; Ryan does play to his ideals (even though his dialogue veers into the cheesy sometimes). Cohen and oddly McClintock had some interesting quirks that kept the book going. The relationship between Bill and Ryan is there, but could've benefited from a much stronger relationship. Fans might like this for what it was trying to be, others would like it simply for being a Bioshock novel, while others might absolutely hate it. For those who aren't fans though, the book might not be too hard to understand since it really spells out the events, regardless of the large amount of POVs.<br /><br />[I decided to give the story 3 stars, even though I felt like giving it half a star more. Its between an OK/Good for me because it wasn't all bad.]

0140277447|3.0|This is the kind of book you don't want to criticize.  It exposes an episode of almost indescribable brutality, that's difficult to read and must have been even more difficult to research and write.  The hell for the residents of Nanking leave the reader shocked and numb after a while.  So much of WWII history, at least in America, is focused on the Nazis and the Holocaust that the horrors of the war in Asia get short shrift, reflecting the undercurrent of racism that pervades US mainstream history.  Iris Chang's contribution is essential, and that it's part of a sadly small body of history makes it all the more necessary.  That Chang committed suicide, apparently to some degree by being so affected by this story, makes it all the more poignant, and all the more difficult to deal with the book's weaknesses.  However they're there, at least three major gaps.  First, she doesn't really document her sources.  Atrocities are notoriously difficult to accurately document; even much of the European Holocaust history involves guesswork.  But it would have helped the book, and help silence the doubters and naysayers, to footnote where possible.  Second, Chang was a good journalist, but a poor historian.  The lack of historical context in her book is striking.  She makes little effort to describe the background behind the Japanese invasion, and is glaringly ignorant of the conflict between the Communists and the Nationalists, who gave the brunt of the fighting against the Japanese to the Red Army and spent more effort dealing with the warlords for power.  And third, she gives a lot of space to the attempts by Westerners, including Nazi agents in China, to protest the brutality, but the irony of having a supposedly positive role played by a Nazi agent close enough to have the ear of the Nazi high command in Germany gets no examination. It's a valuable, earnest, moving book, but the rape of Nanking still sadly awaits a more serious historical treatment.

0452270073|4.0|Regeneration - Pat Barker<br />4 stars<br /><br />...... spoilers ahead if you don't know the historical background............<br /><br />This first book in Pat Barker's WW1 war trilogy begins with the poet Siegfried Sassoon's historical war protest,\\"I am making this statement as an act of willful defiance of military authority, because I believe that the War is being deliberately prolonged by those who have the power to end it....\\". His statement is read by another historical figure, Dr. W.H.R. Rivers, the military physician who treats Sassoon and other shell-shocked soldiers at Craiglockhart War Hospital. Rivers is the central character of this intensely psychological story that delves into the moral, ethical and medical dilemmas of treating mentally wounded officers.<br /><br />There is very little action is this story.  It is, appropriately, as meditative and reflective as an analyst's couch. At the time, psychiatry (talk therapy) was in its infancy. There was little political or social acceptance of mental disorders. Rivers is in the unenviable position of needing to \\"cure\\" otherwise healthy men in order to return them to the front. As readers we become aware of the horrors of the war through his patients' dreams and recollections. It's sad, confronting, depressing, and in its own way, fascinating. In addition to creating an intriguing picture of several 20th century literary figures (Sassoon, Owens and Graves), Barker touches many themes that continue to challenge us today. Rivers called it neurasthenia or shell shock. We speak of post traumatic stress disorder. Siegfried Sassoon was diagnosed with battle fatigue as a means of discrediting his opposition to the conduct of the war. He was also a decorated war hero and a closeted homosexual.<br /><br />Barker's writing style is a bit hard to follow. There is very little scene setting or background information.(In the audio version, changes in scene and characters were sometimes difficult to identify.) Information is conveyed through the conversations or thoughts of the characters. Some previous knowledge of the historical characters in definitely helpful. I started this as an audio book which almost caused me to give up on it completely. Peter Firth has a lovely voice, but he was completely monotonal and hardly varied his voice from character to character.  I found myself dosing off as I listened. Fortunately each time I came awake, the text seemed to be presenting something especially thought provoking. I put the audio version aside. When I began again with the written text, I could hardly put it down. I look forward to reading the rest of the trilogy.rsations or thoughts of the characters. 0962477060|1.0|I was pretty enthusiastic about the premise of this book. It seemed exactly the kind of approach I was looking for. Instead of memorizing a bunch of random seeming details, this book promises to cut through to the underlying *logic*. I'm a logical guy so that sounded great.<br /><br />But what is actually presented was, for me, not very logical at all. The whole book seems to focus on the fact that a C chord looks like an A chord (with a capo on fret #3) and then a G (capo on fret #5) and then an E (#8) and then D (#10). Then if you put those letters together, it spells a word in English: CAGED. Great! Now what's \\"logical\\" about that? I'm not saying it's illogical, but after reading the book I never felt like the logic of that was explained. For example, I could say that if you pick a D, move up two frets, back down 4 frets, up 2 over 1 lower, over one higher, and up 2 frets, it spells DECADE. Is that \\"logical\\"? Maybe. But if I don't explain any logic, it's just a bizarre mnemonic.<br /><br />The book seemed inconsistent in places which definitely erodes the \\"logical\\" impression. For example, on page 96 he says, \\"It makes no sense to attempt to play physically difficult chords like F (E form first position) before your hand is ready for it.\\" But way back on page 5 there is a \\"G Form 5th Pos. C Chord\\" that can't possibly be any easier.<br /><br />This left me with the question what exactly should I play or practice using this book? Each page has a bunch of rambling text and on most pages the right half is filled with chord diagrams. Even when the topic is scales or the vaguely defined \\"lead patterns\\", everything is in fretboard chord diagrams.<br /><br />In short, there is no magic way to learn the guitar. If you are really interested in understanding the fretboard (which is a good sign), you'd be better off memorizing CGDAEBF# (My mnemonic is Cute Girls Don't Always Equal Best Friends). Why? Because the logic of the fretboard really comes from the logic of music (to the extent that music is in any way logical). Far more helpful than this book for deciphering the cryptic patterns of a guitar fretboard were two things: first are on-line resources about music theory (the Wikipedia article on \\"Circle Of Fifths\\" is a more logical and universal resource than this book and explains CGDAEBF#). The second thing, which allows you to relate music theory to the guitar, is memorizing the fretboard. I just don't think there is some hokey shortcut to avoid this. If you don't instantly know what note you're playing at all times, you won't be able to properly appreciate the subtle logic of how the guitar is organized.way logical). B004NSULHM|1.0|Normally I wouldn't even take the time to review an album, but this is just too awful to let slide. I am (or was is perhaps more appropriate) a die-hard Radiohead fan, I have all their albums, EPs, are large number of CD singles, the deluxe editions of In Rainbows and Hail to the Thief, even T-shirts. And I can't think of any other way to put it: this is a woeful album.    The album opens with 'Bloom', an utterly incoherent mess of samples/loops and mumbled, whiny vocals, which pretty much sets the scene for the entire album. I would like to describe the song more accurately, but there is simply no substance to it.    'Morning Mr Magpie' opens with one of the most annoying and poorly mixed guitar riffs I've ever heard, and pretty much continues in the same vein as above - drum loops, mumbled vocals, non-descript lyrics. Listening closely, one can hear some interesting guitar feedback textures around the 2:30 mark, but not nearly interesting enough to redeem such garbage.    'Little by Little' recalls 'Bodysnatchers' from the previous album, but without any focus or energy.    'Feral' is next, except I find it nigh impossible to tell the difference between this track and 'Bloom'. It continues with the same incredibly irritating drum machine and completely incoherent vocalising that is found throughout the album.    'Lotus Flower' - there are almost hints of melody and tune in this one. Unfortunately, the melody (and the bass line) are unremarkable. And don't get me started on the lyrics.    'Codex' is somewhat better, although nowhere remotely as good as a track like 'Sail to the Moon' (Hail to the Thief), the closest comparison I could probably make. It is incredibly repetitive to boot.     'Give up the Ghost' is notable for having an acoustic guitar, although the whole thing sounds like a loop, and it goes nowhere.     As for 'Separator', well, I can't really be bothered anymore. See my comments on 'Bloom' and 'Feral' above. You may as well just listen to 'The Tourist' from OK computer, it is pretty much like they took that track and put it over that god-awful drum loop that they used for almost the entirety of 'King of Limbs' (after removing any hint of melody).    This entire 'album' (if an 8 song demo-quality release can be called that) is a slap in the face to anyone who has ever called themself a Radiohead fan. They could give it away for free and it would still be poor value (unfortunately I eagerly bought the cheaply packaged 'newspaper' version for an exhorbitant amount of money). I can't believe that so many people have given this five stars - although I suspect these are the same people who would give five stars to an album full of Thom Yorke farting into a megaphone.    And a note to those who praise Radiohead for their 'textures' and 'sonic landscapes' (blah blah blah) - drenching every track on the album with reverb does not a 'beautifully atmospheric' album make. Besides, Radiohead are relatively unskilled when it comes to dealing with creating atmospheres (that is, there are people who do it much more successfully) - they only succeeded by placing such effects within the context of well-crafted songs, of which there are none on this album. Some might say the same about Kid A, but at least that album was varied, interesting and had some real energy to it. This is simply the sound of a band that can't be bothered trying anymore.

B000VIP5L0|4.0|Sleep, much like Kyuss were a pioneering force in Stoner Rock, who unfortunately broke up before they really got their due, and have since moved on to form the basis of other important bands.<br /><br />Their second album Holy Mountain (also known as Sleep's Holy Mountain) is a touchstone of the genre, the sort of must have album that everyone who likes this type of music either owns or should own.<br /><br />The production value is absolutely minimal, the lyrics are pretty poor sang with no great musicality and the album is absolutely bursting at the seems with Black Sabbath homage.<br /><br />Despite all of this, Sleep's Holy Mountain is a good album, with big riffs and contains the majority of the band's best loved songs. The band's big riffs mixed with loose jam-feel style works wonders on this record. The 1970s influence comes through on everything from songwriting to the tonal choices and what amplifiers they use.<br /><br />For most people, the reputation of the album alone is enough to warrant interest. If you are unsure whether or not the album is for you, listen to the album opener `Dragonaut,' and that should give you a very good indication of the direction of the record.
6304907648|4.0|A slick, stylish, psychological thriller about serial killers, Copycat (1995) isn't very realistic, but it is quite entertaining, providing a nice mix of suspense, action, drama, and mystery.  The story may be somewhat predictable and clich&eacute;d, but the strong performances by Sigorney Weaver, Holly Hunter, and William McNamara, and some surprises and twists, result in a film that is worth seeing.<br /><br />Expert on serial killers Helen Hudson (Weaver) is almost killed by Daryll Lee Cullum (Harry Connick Jr.), an escaped serial killer that she helped send to prison.  The trauma of almost being hanged in a toilet stall, causes her to become an agoraphobic recluse who never leaves her apartment.  When a serial killer appears to be at work in San Francisco, Hudson contacts the police to offer advice, which leads to her reluctantly becoming involved in the case, working with detectives Mary Jane `MJ' Monahan (Hunter), and Reuben Goetz (Dermont Mulroney).<br /><br />Hudson surmises that the killer is actually copying the modus operandi of various infamous serial killers from the past, such as the Boston Strangler, David Berkowitz, the Hillside Strangler, and Jeffery Dahmer.  Predictably, Hudson becomes a target of the killer, and things start to get very personal.  Her computer is hacked, her home invaded, and those close to her are threatened.  The crisis causes the authorities to make a deal with the devil, as Dr. Hudson videoconferences with Cullum who is behind bars in prison, to get a lead to the copycat killer's identity.  Harry Connick Jr. isn't onscreen much, but he is rather chilling playing a stereotypical psychotic type, who kind of looks like Earnest P. Worrell.<br /><br />Copycat is guilty of being overdramatic, and the agoraphobic attacks are one element that are a bit hard to take, as Helen Hudson kind of fluctuates between being capable and proactive, to being vulnerable and too scared to move.  Sigorney Weaver pulls it off well, bringing an edge and believability to her performance, even though the situations are sometimes ridiculous.  William McNamara gives a gripping performance as Peter Foley, a cold hearted killer who has a chameleon-like ability to adapt his appearance and personality, to accomplish his grisly deeds.  At the finish, Foley steps nicely into the spotlight, displaying signs of madness, without going too far overboard.<br /><br />At 123 minutes, the movie runs a little long, and might have benefited from trimming some the periphery drama, like Monahan's romantic relationship with another cop (Will Patton).  While the storyline humanizes the character, and gives Holly Hunter a chance to dig deeper, it really doesn't add significantly to the central story.<br /><br />The DVD release features both widescreen and full screen presentations, and a very insightful and on topic audio commentary by director Jon Amiel, who among many things, reveals that the original screenplay was reworked to feature two women in the leading roles.  Obviously a fortuitous decision, as Hunter and Weaver have some great scenes together.  Copycat is a very slick crime drama that quickly draws you in, and then takes you on a dark and uneasy journey.o her performance, even though the situations are sometimes ridiculous. These 'phones are almost identical to the MDR-V6, but there are differences that go beyond the cosmetic. She made her decision between Edward and Jacob. Now she must follow the consequences like everybody else. Unfortunately, for a woman who is too unwilling to kill off anyone with a sense of decency (ten werewolves, seven good vampires, Bella, and all of her human friends and family survived Eclipse while none of the bad guys did), she can't even make an ending book all that good. Making some regrettable decisions earlier in the story would have obviously made a more realistic and less strange ending entirely possible.

0060930349|2.0|I decided to read this book to refresh my memory of American History before teaching it again this fall.  I have read numerous books on the subject so I was looking for something that was interesting, yet informative.  At first, it seemed to fulfill my goals.  However, early on I began to get a bad vibe because I noticed small mistakes cropping up that prompted me to sense that some of Johnson's research had been lazy.  He also used terms that stuck out like referring to the Confederacy as the \\"Confederation\\" or the Union army as the \\"Unionist\\" army.  What American historian would make a mistake like that?  When I checked the back cover I discovered that Johnson is apparently British which might explain some of these faulty terms.         I decided to press on because the book is interesting and I do not mind historians giving opinions.  (Although I will always wonder if his conservative bent would have gotten intolerable as the book moved into the post-WWII period.)  I have read enough American History to recognize opinions when I read them.  It concerns me that many of the readers might not distinguish his opinions from facts.  I also did not mind his emphasis of the role of religion on American History.  You don't get that much in books like this so it was a welcome addition.  However, it does strike me as a theme that was included because it is a particular interest of Johnson, not so much because it was important to add to historical understanding.        My big problem, and the reason I had to stop reading the book (something I very seldom do) was the ridiculous historical errors which finally wore me down and made me wonder how many mistakes I was not catching.  I stopped in the middle of the Civil War because his coverage was filled with errors. Don't get me wrong, I do not expect an historian to be an expert on everything in American History.  However, Johnson's mistakes were laughable.  For example, he describes the First Battle of Bull Run as a Confederate offensive against Washington.  He then confuses Joseph Johnston with Albert Sidney Jonhston.  He obviously thinks that Stonewall Jackson was in command of the Army of Northern Virginia until \\"shot in the back\\" by Rebel soldiers.  His coverage of the Battle of Gettysburg has several howlers. At this point I could not take it any more.  To make matters worse, either Johnson is not good at spelling and grammar or the book had a terrible editor.  Either way, there are some embarassing mistakes in this area as well.        I am upset that most of the reviews are very positive which is scary because either these people did not realize they were exposed to numerous errors or they don't care just so long as the book is interesting.

B003Y5H59W|5.0|It's crucial that you get to like Steve Rogers when he was only this 98-pound weakling, crucial that you grow to care for this runty, scrawny, asthmatic kid who persists in standing up for himself and for others, never mind that this usually rewards him with bruises and lumps. It's 1943, in wartime America, and Steve Rogers yearns to do his part, if the military would only allow him to. Five times he's tried to enlist only to be summarily rejected as 4F. Meanwhile, his best friend James \\"Bucky\\" Barnes is a sergeant in the U.S. Army, cutting a dashing figure, shining the ladies, bullying those who'd been bullying Steve. You note Steve's lack of resentment over this, and like him for it.<br /><br />CAPTAIN AMERICA: THE FIRST AVENGER is the one I'd been looking forward to the most this year, and I'm pleased as punch to say that this movie did not tank, that it in fact is fairly bloody brilliant. I enjoyed THOR and X-MEN: FIRST CLASS, but CAPTAIN AMERICA is the best Marvel flick since IRON MAN and it gets you charged up for THE AVENGERS movie. Chris Evans - damn.<br /><br />Steve Rogers's impassioned speech to Bucky is overheard by Dr. Abraham Erskine, a kindly German scientist working for the U.S. government. Dr. Erskine heads a top secret government program involving the creation of the super-soldier, and on these shores he's looking for a candidate to be his inaugural test subject (he'd had an earlier candidate elsewhere but that didn't work out so well; more on that in a bit). Dr. Erskine sees past Steve's sickly frame. He's intrigued with Steve's spirit. He gets him enlisted in the U.S. Army.<br /><br />Compared to the rest of the film, the first third segment is less action-packed, but that doesn't equate to dull or slow. We track Steve Rogers' time in boot camp and we take in his struggles. He tires easy. He's always finishing last. The camp's gruff director, Colonel Phillips (Tommy Lee Jones, hogging all the good lines), is highly skeptical and takes Dr. Erskine to task for Rogers' presence. But we note Rogers' quiet diligence and doggedness, and these strong character moments provide the building blocks for making what happens to him later so resonating and gratifying.<br /><br />If you're caught up on Marvel Comics continuity, then you're up to snuff on what happens next. Steve Rogers undergoes Erskine's treatment which galvanizes change on an awesome cellular scale. I think the best CG work done by the film's f/x crew, hands down, is in the shrinking and scrawnying of Chris Evans and making it look seamless. Steve Rogers as the beneficiary of the super-soldier serum is a picture in bold contrast, a foot taller and now rippling with muscles. But this is Chris Evans in real, the guy having worked his tail off to get this buff (even though Chris Hemsworth probably still points at him and smirks).<br /><br />If you don't know your Captain America history, I'll just mention that an act of sabotage renders Steve Rogers the world's one and only super soldier. Which gets us to an inspired plot thread which isn't part of Captain America lore. And, fine, here's a ***SPOILERS alert*** for the rest of this paragraph. With Rogers the only one of his kind, Colonel Phillips has no use for him, and so Steve Rogers is deployed to USO tour appearances, putting on a show in the home front with chorus girls and shilling war bonds as the military mascot \\"Captain America.\\" Like a good soldier, Steve goes along with it, but he wishes he could do more. We know that Cap will join the war soon enough, but, in the meantime, his time on stage just fills me with cinematic joy. I absolutely love that he gets his own awesome show jingle, \\"Star Spangled Man,\\" with music by Alan Menken. I also like how the film addresses the inherent cheesiness of the traditional Captain America costume, which is what he wears on these shows. Cap's later costume re-design would be more practical, less shiny, less tights.<br /><br />Chris Evans inhabits his role with conviction, imbues Steve Rogers with spunk and an appealing sweetness, and I'm not worried any more about how he might hold up against Robert Downey, Jr. and Samuel Jackson in THE AVENGERS movie. He just proved to me that he can command the screen. Fine actors like Tommy Lee Jones, Stanley Tucci, and Hugo Weaving add so much to the story. If you could point at the heart of the story, it may be the almost father-son relationship between Steve Rogers and Tucci's Dr. Erskine. Tucci isn't in the film much, but he leaves a defining mark. I was concerned about how the Red Skull would come off, but I should've known Hugo Weaving would make it work. His Nazi commandant, Johann Schmidt a.k.a. the Red Skull, is a convincing villain and, incidentally, a failed past test subject of Erskine's. As a guilty pleasure, I'm glad the biochemist Arnim Zola is here as well. Or, I dunno, maybe I'm fond of him because Ookla the Mok's \\"Super Skrull\\" song references him. (\\"He's endorsing RC Cola and cruising chicks with Arnim Zola.\\")<br /><br />Parts of Steve's origin are snatched from Marvel's Ultimate universe (portraying Bucky as someone in Steve's age bracket, for one thing). For fans of Marvel's cinematic continuity, Dominic Cooper riffs Robert Downey, Jr. a bit as Tony Stark's rakish genius industrialist father, Howard Stark. I wish Hayley Atwell as British Agent Peggy Carter were given more to do, but I suspect Atwell will reappear in future films as Agent 13, Sharon Carter. And if you were expecting Sgt. Fury and his Howling Commandos to show up, you're sort of right. The action is terrific, and maybe it's terrific because the story had taken the time to invest in its lead character. These aren't just tatters of action sequences. There's a compelling thru arc to tie everything together.<br /><br />But, okay, one thing nagged me. There's something jarringly anachronistic about the presence of Hydra and its super science. The strongest emotional impression I have to Cap is that he was a World War II hero who fought the Nazis and later became a tormented man-out-of-time. This movie removes the first part by replacing the Nazis with Hydra goons. And, although Hydra is supposed to be affiliated with them storm troopers - serving as Hitler's \\"deep science division\\" - it's not the same. \\"Heil Hydra!\\" just doesn't ring true. And Nazis always did make the best big bads.<br /><br />CAPTAIN AMERICA: THE FIRST AVENGER gives it up with that old-fashioned wartime nostalgia, with a grip of strong performances, with an indomitable legend to whom Chris Evans does justice. This movie has heart. Nothing embodies that more than the sequence in which the new Steve Rogers is in pursuit of a spy who tries to distract him by tossing a boy into the drink. About to dive in the water, Steve hears the boy briskly yell at him: \\"I can swim. Go get him!\\" That bit of bravado from the kid generated the loudest cheers from the theater audience.<br /><br />As per norm, stick around until after the closing credits. If what you see doesn't excite you... then you're probably executives over at DC Comics.nviction, imbues Steve Rogers with spunk and an appealing sweetness, and I'm not worried any more about how he might hold up against Robert Downey, Jr. and Samuel Jackson in THE AVENGERS movie. B003XRDYX2|5.0|Columbia has truly gone the extra mile with this one. \\"The Original Mono Recordings\\" is a truly monumental release, as it is the first time that Dylan's first eight albums are available in, as the title states, their original mono pressings. The sound on these records is simply glorious. Anyone who owns the SACD stereo remasters of these records from 2004 can now discard of them; while they were certainly the best those recordings sounded up to that point, the mono recordings are more natural sounding, as well as having more punch and warmth to them.<br /><br />The acoustic records benefit the most. Releasing them in stereo was a bad idea to begin with, making for a very awkward listening experience. The mono recordings make you feel like Bob is in the room with you. They now have the same warmth and intimacy on cd that they did on record 50 years ago. The electric records aren't as noticeable an improvement, but certain songs do jump out at you. \\"Like a Rolling Stone\\" was meant to be heard in mono, with every sound coming right at you and kicking you in the ass, rather than taking its time to come together on the stereo records. \\"Visions of Johanna\\" has a similar effect, captivating you right from the opening strains. A select few do indeed sound better in stereo form (\\"Most Likely...\\" sounds surprisingly weakened) but the difference for the most part is quite negligible, and the fact these are, in fact, the way Dylan and co. meant for them to be heard makes it a pretty easy choice for most fans.<br /><br />The packaging is superb. The slip cases are beautiful recreations of the original pressings, and the accompanying booklet is brimming with a fine essay and rare photographs. As stated, Columbia did not slack in making this a special effort. \\"The Original Mono Recordings\\" is a wholly worthwhile purchase, and essential for Dylan scholars.

0743297334|5.0|I have a few really \\"favorite\\" writers.  Ernest Hemingway is on the shortest end of that shortlist, so... you are warned...<br /><br />I read this book for the first time, about three and a half years ago.  It was one of the first of my new period of intense reading, and it totally changed my focus as a reader, and a writer.  I just reread it aloud, with my best friend, and walked away with a completely renewed appreciation.<br /><br />As an aside, let me say, if you have never read a great book, aloud, with someone you love, with wine, and voices, and laughter, then you are missing a great opportunity to experience literature.  It is one of my favorite things in the world.<br /><br />Now to the book that changed all of literature:<br /><br />In this book we have the embodiment of the artist reinventing the medium.  Hemingway is throwing away, right before our eyes, so many literary conventions that we lose track.  What emerges is a new - completely new, way of writing.<br /><br />If you are writer, or aspire to write, and you come to Hemingway, you spend the whole time thinking, \\"How does he do that?\\" and your answer, of course, is \\"Hemingway was braver than you are.\\"  He was not afraid of reducing word count, of under-writing, and these fears bury a writer.<br /><br />Gone, largely, is plot as a straightjacket of narrative.  There is a story here, but it is not a story that \\"goes\\" anywhere, or begins, or ends.  These are just moments, and people.  Often they are fragmented, and incomplete, or incongruous, but they are \\"real\\" in a way that is difficult to achieve, and nearly impossible to achieve with the sparse toolbox Hemingway leaves himself.<br /><br />The Author gives us the realism of his favorite writers, and does them all better.  He is famously attributed with the quote, \\"....nobody's going to get me in any ring with Mr. Tolstoy...\\" but I think he underestimates himself.  By throwing away all the dusty trappings, he beats Tolstoy at his own game.<br /><br />Gone are the adjectives, the long, subordinate clauses that so many of us use, and over use, when we write, gone completely, is the convention of \\"telling\\" in favor of showing, and so much of that showing, for Hemingway, just boils down to dialogue.<br /><br />More than any other tool, Hemingway tells us a story with naked dialogue, stripped of adjectives and often stripped even of attribution.  You can see him cutting, taking away words that are unnecessary, and leaving the reader only what is left, to interpret as one would interpret a real experience.<br /><br />There is plenty of emotion in Hemingway, but it comes without a heavy handed authorship, and is so masterfully done, that is often missed, and its absence mocked by the critics.  His characters feel, care, love and fear, like any others, but they wear the masks of real life.<br /><br />Finally, on characters - This book has a cast of wonderful, memorable, characters.  They are well, if sparsely, drawn, and perhaps brought out best by the convention of out-loud reading.  It is easy to imagine Hemingway reading these passages aloud to himself, or to Hadley, to get the cadence right.<br /><br />The writing world is full of hack writers (most notably Stephen King) who crow about having learned the lessons of Hemingway, and then completely fail to produce a page of worthy prose.  If you have not discovered the source - The Sun Also Rises - do yourself a favor, and find a friend to read it with.<br /><br />There is certainly more to Hemingway than this book, which I would argue is not even his best, but here is where it really begins, for Hemingway, and for so much of 20th century American literature./>Gone are the adjectives, the long, subordinate clauses that so many of us use, and over use, when we write, gone completely, is the convention of \\"telling\\" in favor of showing, and so much of that showing, for Hemingway, just boils down to dialogue.<br /><br />More than any other tool, Hemingway tells us a story with naked dialogue, stripped of adjectives and often stripped even of attribution.  You can see him cutting, taking away words that are unnecessary, and leaving the reader only what is left, to interpret as one would interpret a real experience.<br /><br />There is plenty of emotion in Hemingway, but it comes without a heavy handed authorship, and is so masterfully done, that is often missed, and its absence mocked by the critics.  His characters feel, care, love and fear, like any others, but they wear the masks of real life.<br /><br />Finally, on characters - This book has a cast of wonderful, memorable, characters.  They are well, if sparsely, drawn, and perhaps brought out best by the convention of out-loud reading.  It is easy to imagine Hemingway reading these passages aloud to himself, or to Hadley, to get the cadence right.<br /><br />The writing world is full of hack writers (most notably Stephen King) who crow about having learned the lessons of Hemingway, and then completely fail to produce a page of worthy prose.  If you have not discovered the source - The Sun Also Rises - do yourself a favor, and find a friend to read it with.<br /><br />There is certainly more to Hemingway than this book, which I would argue is not even his best, but here is where it really begins, for Hemingway, and for so much of 20th century American literature.

B004LUHQ1G|5.0|Of course, with my 5-star review, you can tell that I like the songs on this CD, but others have reviewed the songs quite thoroughly, so I am opting to talk about the production. Full disclosure: I have known Butch Vig for many years--in fact I co-own a studio just down the street from his recently closed Smart Studios in Madison, Wisconsin. So I am a bit biased, but Butch is an analog wizard. He plays the analog recording chain like Orli Shaham plays Scriabin on the piano, which is to say with a virtuoso's skill. Check it out: try listening a couple of times to the actual CD, rather than to a 128kbps MP3 file on your iPod (though it sounds good on the iPod too). This disc showcases great miking technique, mixing, production values and, perhaps most of all, the use of analog equipment as artforms in themselves. If you appreciate good songs, you'll like this disc; if you appreciate recording as an artform, you will LOVE this disc.

0316056863|5.0|Bossypants is a book that I appreciated on so many levels, because it works on so many levels.  As the product description blurb here says, it is a messy book, so don't come at it expecting a nice, ordered progression of Fey's life story.  Yet, this messiness really works for it.  Reading it is like sitting down for a cup of coffee and having a chat with Fey, just listening while one thought leads to another, resulting a sort of stream-of-consciousness to the narrative.    There's a reason why Tina Fey is so engaging.  She is witty, self-deprecating, and smart.  All of those come through in this book.  However, though she is a comic genius, she also seems to be infused with a real humbleness.  She never tries to pretend that she's perfect or that her life is perfect.  She readily admits to obnoxious behavior and moments of stupidity.  When you read this, you can't help but feel like she's one of your good friends, that you have a relationship in which you're comfortable admitting your flaws to one another.  The fact that she's so downright human is a big part of Fey's appeal.  She's not trying to be a princess or a super glamorous girl.  Instead, she's the way most of us women secretly are: relieved when we can put on our messy sweats, scrape our hair back into a ponytail, and flop down on the couch.  I think this really comes through in the hilarious section about doing photo shoots.      Fey's determination is also very appealing to me.  She has a lot of guts, as the book shows.  She's taken risks and fought to make her way in a world that is often uncomfortable, if not downright inhospitable to women.  When I read about her experiences in the business, in how she has dealt with the notion that people aren't interested in women comics, I really admired her resolve.  There is a clear feminist message here, and Fey perfectly illustrates how, while overt sexism is perhaps becoming a thing of the past, there is still that patronizing, insidious kind of sexism that isalive and well.  I'm thrilled that women like Fey are proving that they can be every bit as funny as men, and that their humor can appeal to a broad swath of the population.  It's thrilling to see someone using her brains, wit, and savvy to get ahead, rather than falling back on tired stereotypes of women as toys.    The motherhood sections of the book are also priceless.  As the mother of a daughter, I could really relate to the \\"prayer\\" Fey includes.  It's so hilarious that it had me laughing until I teared up.  I agreed with every single sentiment, and loved the way Fey can take motherly anxieties and find a way to laugh about them.  Her struggle with her desire to have another baby conflicting with her career ambitions is also particularly poignant.  This is a very real struggle for many people.  I felt a real rush of affection for her as I read her section about breast feeding.  This can be a real taboo subject with women, and I was heartened to see Fey take a clear stance on it, exposing it for the struggle that it truly can be.      But much of the book is like this: Fey takes the gloss off a subject, shows it's ugly underbelly, and then pokes fun at it.  Like the rest of us, she can be neurotic, tactless, cranky, and stressed, she just really, really good at finding a way to laugh about it.  As a result, Bossypants is good therapy.
B000002X1O|4.0|Baron Von Tollbooth & the Chrome Nun features a style that I believe is quite different from previous Paul Kantner albums, and seriously a drastic step away from anything Jefferson Airplane or Jefferson Starship ever wrote.   It's not necessarily a fantastic departure either.  The music sounds partially aimless or undeveloped sometimes, and just severely dated in other areas.  An inconsistent affair.  The highlights are two magnificent songs that I will mention below, and three or four other really good ones.    \\"Your Mind Has Left Your Body\\" is perhaps the most unique song on the entire album.  It takes elements of country music in the guitar playing and combines it with hypnotic and dreamy singing.  The vocal melody definitely reminds me of the guy from the Incredible String Band.  I doubt it's an imitation though, since the Incredible String Band already had their moment in the sun long before this album was released.  To be perfectly honest, it's hard to rate a song like this and I can't tell you if I actually enjoy it or not.  I *think* I do.  Nothing about the song really does a whole lot besides linger on and on, but thanks to the lyrics, the feeling of adventure is still quite evident.  That and the country-inspired guitar work is the greatest aspect of the song.  Normally I don't believe you have to be on drugs to get the full effect of a song but... well, haha.    \\"Sketches of China\\" has an unavoidably depressing feeling thanks to Grace's vocals.  This song hits me pretty hard because you can tell it's designed to destroy someones mental state of mind... or perhaps not.  The lyrics wouldn't necessarily indicate such inner devastation.  In fact, the melody is supposed to give the listener an uplifting and positive feeling.  As far as I know, it's about the child Paul and Grace made together.  The harmonies are extraordinary though.  Absolutely the very best I've ever heard the two of them create.  The guitar solos are enough to send me to a faraway land aswell.   Well, I can't sit here and talk about THIS song all day when there's flowers outside that need watered and other songs that need discussed!      \\"Across the Board\\" is another Grace Slick highlight.  Describing the brilliance of the vocal melody would be impossible, or rather, would make me look like a fanboy who can't listen to music objectively.  Not for me!  Let's just say, this is yet another terrific performance that takes advantage of her incredible range.  Somehow her range becomes even better as the song progresses.  \\"Flowers of the Night\\" has another military march-like vocal melody (as I prefer to call it- perhaps there's a more appropriate name for those type of vocal melodies) that resembles a good portion of Paul Kantner's Sunfighter album.  Not a highlight for me, but perhaps others can find some appeal in it.  It's a major low point of the album for me and if it wasn't for the decent guitar solo at the end I would dismiss it without hesitation.     \\"White Boy\\" has another underwhelming vocal melody that does nothing interesting.  The lyrics seem to focus on questioning our existence and human accomplishments concerning white people, and while interesting, it's not enough to make the mediocre vocal melody worth sitting through.  A song that I believe wasn't worked on entirely because the melody should have matched the quality of the lyrics but unfortunately, it doesn't.   However, it's not a bad song.  I just wish more attention was given to the actual vocal melody.  \\"Harp Tree Lament\\" is really good though, thanks entirely to the vocal melody and nothing else. 0938045695|4.0|Okay I know Pavel has some very rabid fans and detractors out there.  He seems to polarize people right off into either camp, so with that in mind I will try to be as unbiased as possible.  Enter the Kettlebell is a great book mainly for beginners.  The book itself is well made with quality paper and good illustrations.  Some consideration went into the design of it, and personally that's a big plus in my book (no pun intended).  Color photos, glossy heavy-duty pages, and good sized print makes it very easy on the eyes.  Zero complaints on the physical properties.<br /><br />As for the content well this I think is where people can start having their differences.  Pavel pulls no punches.  He loves kettlebells, and makes no bones about them being *the* way to train for strength, and all but says its the manly way to build functional strength.  I personally like that spin - it makes you feel part of something and gets you excited to go lift.  Problem is sometimes its laid on a bit too thick, and he sometimes turns it into a sales pitch for other products. (this is why it does not get five stars)<br /><br />The program and exercises themselves are top-notch with a lot of detail to explaining them and showing how to not do them.  This is something people have complained about - that he wastes too much time showing silly pictures of doing it wrong.  I know that many of us knows to not do some of this stuff but I think its great he's trying to cover the bases.  Too many fitness books just show pictures of how it's done right.  In any event I would still recommend seeing some videos of how the exercises are performed.  It is a great book, and if you are new to lifting kettlebells, or just want to see how Pavel and his followers do it is worth the investment.

B003Y5H59W|5.0|A good bit of this film is a superhero-origins story, in which we follow Steve Rogers (Chris Evans), a plain-featured young man of about 20 years old who is a classic \\"90-pound weakling,\\" as he tries five times to enlist in the Army during the early part of World War II. He is consistently rated as \\"4F\\" because of his size and various health issues, including asthma. Inside that small, frail body, however, resides outsized courage, honor, loyalty and persistence. During Steve's fifth trip to the Army recruiters, those virtues in Steve draw the attention of Dr. Abraham Erskine (Stanley Tucci), a German scientist who escaped the Nazis and is working on a top-secret program to develop super soldiers. Colonel Chester Phillips (Tommy Lee Jones), who is in charge of the program, wants to use a soldier who is bigger, stronger and has more training for the first human experiment with the Dr. Erskine's super-soldier formula, but the doctor strongly disagrees. He says the other soldier is a bully, but Steve is a good man. It is crucial that anyone receiving the formula be of good character, because the formula enhances the existing personality traits of whoever receives it. Steve would become even more of a good person, but a bully could become a villain.<br /><br />The experiment is a success, but immediately after Steve is transformed into a handsome, ripped, perfect specimen of manhood, a Nazi spy assassinates Dr. Erskine and steals the formula. Though Steve manages to stop the assassin from escaping, in his first act of heroism as a newly minted superhero, the flask breaks in the process. Without a sample of the formula, it cannot be replicated because the doctor never wrote down the whole formula. Steve is now one of a kind. Unfortunately, the military can't think of anything to do with him other than turn him into a US-flag-wrapped peddler of war bonds appearing in USO shows with chorus girls, until Steve is sent abroad. He finds himself entertaining the recently decimated troops of Col. Phillips and is horrified to discover that his best friend James Buchanan \\"Bucky\\" Barnes (Sebastian Stan) has been captured with 400 other Allied soldiers and is being held prisoner in a massively defended fortress deep in enemy lines. Col. Phillips insists he would lose far more men than he could save going after his captured men, and refuses to do anything. But the colonel's assistant, a female military officer, Peggy Carter (Hayley Atwell), encourages Steve to fulfill his destiny as the super-soldier Dr. Erskine created him to be by staging a one-man rescue raid.<br /><br />On every level this film is outstanding. It is directed by the talented Joe Johnston (Jurassic Park III, [[ASIN:B000031EGV Jumanji]] ). The screenplay by Christopher Markus and Stephen McFeely (the writing team best known for the three [[ASIN:B000E8M0VA Chronicles of Narnia]] films) is an excellent adaptation, staying true in every important way to the world of a comic-book icon with a 70-year history of stories in Marvel Comics since the Captain's first appearance in 1941.<br /><br />All of the actors are terrific, but the star, Chris Evans (who played the comic-book superhero, Johnny Storm, the Human Torch, in the two [[ASIN:B00005JNTU Fantastic Four]] films), is superb. Evans gives a depth and breadth to his performance that is remarkable for any genre, but especially for a superhero film. In his capable hands, Steve is endearingly humble, yet enduringly determined to have a chance to contribute to the worldwide struggle against the Nazis in the beginning, origins part of the film. And after the transformation, he compellingly presents Steve as a fascinating contradiction of a relentless, manly warrior who is still emotionally innocent and nave.<br /><br />Steve's relationship with Peggy, as his romantic interest in the film, has significant barriers to their connection that makes it both amusing and exciting to watch. She is his superior officer and, for a young man who has barely even had a conversation with a woman before her, it takes more bravery than going to war for him to aspire to a relationship with a woman like Peggy. She is not only gorgeous, but a formidable warrior in her own right. What I found as intriguing as the romantic potential between these two, however, is the fact that they bring out the best in each other. Peggy gives Steve the inspiration to seize his destiny as a super-warrior when the colonel and other leaders have ordered him to sit out the war, and Steve's innate sensitivity and goodness soften the shell of cynicism Peggy has developed around her heart in the harsh, man's world of the Army.<br /><br />The incredibly versatile Stanley Tucci ([[ASIN:B000J103PC The Devil Wears Prada]], The Lovely Bones) is terrific as Dr. Erskine. Hugo Weaving (who played V in [[ASIN:B000FS9FCG V for Vendetta]] and Agent Smith in [[ASIN:B000P0J0AQ The Matrix]] movies) is his usual brilliant self as the villain Red Skull. Anytime he appears in anything it is a gift, but he is particularly skilled as a comic-book super villain. As for Tommy Lee Jones, he was simply made for the part of Colonel Chester Phillips. Sebastian Stan (Carter Baizen on [[ASIN:B000W6ZUUC Gossip Girl]]) is a convincing choice as Bucky. He and Chris Evans have excellent buddy chemistry as best friends since childhood. All of the other supporting actors are great, too, including Neal McDonough ([[ASIN:B0000ACOXL Traitor]]) as Timothy 'Dum Dum' Dugan, Derek Luke ([[ASIN:B00005JLRZ Antwone Fisher]]) as Gabe Jones, Kenneth Choi ([[ASIN:B001BP4K22 Street Kings]]) as Jim Morita, and JJ Feild (Northanger Abbey) as James Montgomery Falsworth.<br /><br />Finally, the special effects in the action scenes, aided by computer-generated imagery (CGI), are absolutely breathtaking, making this movie a must-see on the big screen. There is also one other CGI effect that I found awe-inspiring. The real, buffed Chris Evans is trimmed down via CGI to the pre-formula Steve.  \\"It's pretty amazing,\\" Evans told Reuters. \\"They took shape out of my jaw line, they shrunk my skeleton, and they made my shoulders less broad.\\"  They certainly did. The skinny Steve has the body of a prepubescent boy. As for the actual physique of Chris Evans--he went through a specialized training program to put on 15 pounds of muscle on a physique that was already quite ripped.<br /><br />Among the multiple feature films already done on the Captain, this should delight fans as a standout contribution.rsation with a woman before her, it takes more bravery than going to war for him to aspire to a relationship with a woman like Peggy. Saturday is laundry/clean car.    Sunday is free.    As far as zones go, I have five zones based on the calendar.  I marked which zones I need to focus on per week.  I have combined areas, so zone 1 is \\"entrance, porch, laundry room\\", etc.  Zone 2 is kitchen.  Zone 3 is bathrooms.  Zone 4 is bedrooms/closets.  Zone 5 is family rooms/hallway.  Whichever zone I am scheduled to do on the week is dealt with on Thursday/Friday.  Zone cleaning involves deep cleaning/details, baseboards, the more \\"nitty gritty things\\".  I am only talking a half hour-45 min or so, no more. Breaking up my routine over the course of the week has been helpful.  Flylady is motivating, and I love the \\"control/housekeeping journal\\".

I would recommend any ofthem, but \\"Discovery\\" is a discovery to celebrate.

So Jen embarks on the quest to replace the shard of the Dark Crystal, meeting another surviving Gelfling (Kira) who also survived the extermination of the Skeksis, and restore good to their world.    Seeing a movie like this is rather surprising today, considering that it took five years to complete it.  Today everything is all digital, but in those days it was all by hand of complex puppets, make up, costume design, and landscapes.  The special features are of great detail of how they came up with the story, designing the sets and elaborate costumes.  Sometimes we forget just how hard people work at these things and how far we have come in order to make these outstanding creatures.  Also, as the Skeksis and all things within their castle are so hideous and ugly, this was a true visual horror movie.  It was quite a departure from Jim Henson's previous works with the Muppets (even though that was/is whathe is most famous for).  Today kids may not quite understand what is going on, as these things were done by hand rather than with CGI, but it was more of an appreciation for how movie magic is made today.

I will have to say though is these certainly are not worth $3 extra in terms of price. Ezpdf has some issues at the time of writing this review opening certain password protected PDFs. Some password protected pdfs do not open. Also large pdfs open with discernable lag and pages turn sluggishly. Repligo is much much faster in this regard and has opened all pdfs I have thrown at it. Again this does not justify the $3 extra. What makes up this price difference for me is the relative clarity of the displayed pdfs visually. Scanned documents have a much cleaner, smoother font in repligo and much easier on your eyes. This is particularly obvious in documents that have a small font. So if you have plans to spend long hours reading scanned PDFs in particular I would recommend buying repligo, else for light usage ezpdf is a good option with a few useful annotations thrown in.

B000PKX2KA|2.0|I just received this receiver to use with Delorme Street Atlas. My first test (last night on a trip to a chamber music concert in Vermont) of this was an utter failure: when you bring up Street Atlas and turn on the GPS, the system collapses -- blue screen of death or just hangs. Every time. This is an up-to-date Windows XP system running on a Toshiba NB305 netbook, 2 GB memory. Fortunately, I'd also brought my old Garmin GPSMap 60, which got me there and back.    This morning, I did a little Googling (interesting how that's become a verb) and may have found an answer. If you are having the same problem I am, go into the Device Manager (from an account with administrator privileges) with the GPS unit plugged in, under Ports (COM and LPT), you will find the Prolific USB-to-Serial Comm Port. If you right-click it and click 'Properties' and then click the 'Port Settings' tab, you will find a setting for 'Flow control'. It's 'None' by default. Change it to 'Hardware'. Since doing that, I've done one test and for the first time, the system didn't fall over. As an old operating system developer, I think they are getting buffer overruns without flow control, scribbling over random memory, which will kill the system dead.    Once I've tested this more extensively, I will update this review. If it works, great. If not, it's going back.    7/27/2011 update:    The workaround described in my original review a few days ago seems to be reliable. No more OS crashes. It's absurd that they send this thing out with default driver settings that crash XP. I'm getting some use out of the device, but I'm a retired computer professional who has done a lot of OS and driver programming, and so had the instincts to recognize a promising fix when I turned it up by searching. What about an ordinary user who hasn't devoted his/her life to computer arcana?

140053271X|5.0|I purchased the Kindle (Wi-Fi) and the original Nook (the B&W one) and tried them out during the return period. While there were things about the Kindle I really liked (features that other reviewers on the Kindle site had written), the one thing that made me choose the Nook was the ability to borrow and read ebooks from my local library. Haven't been back to the library since!<br /><br />Now here comes the Nook Simple Touch Reader (Nook STR). I got one via pre-order when they came out in early June.  It has the same e-ink Pearl screen as the Kindle, so the screen contrast is no longer an issue. And wow, has the user interface improved over the original Nook! (which I thought was clunky) The entire e-ink Pearl screen has full touchscreen capability. On both the Kindle and the original Nook, I felt myself wanting to touch the screen to select a book to read, instead of using the buttons (on the Kindle) or the LCD touchscreen (on the original Nook) to highlight and select the desired ebook. On the Nook STR, the Library screen shows icons for all the ebooks loaded in the device (both library and purchased). Touch the icon, and the ebook opens. Turning the page? Touch the screen on the right side of the page (to go forward) or the left side (to go back). Or swipe your finger across the screen as in flipping a physical book page. Or use the buttons built into the frame. Want to page quickly through a book? Hold down the page turning button. Need a word definition? Touch and hold on the word and a dictionary pops up. The user interface resembles that of an iPhone or iPad, with the icons, virtual keyboard and the physical home button. It even has the \\"slide to unlock\\" feature like the iPhone. The touchscreen on the Nook STR is infrared (it senses the heat of your finger, again like iPhone), so a light touch works just fine. I found that on the original Nook, sometimes it would take multiple touches on the LCD screen for the device to recognize a command.<br /><br />In response to the reviews about the touchscreen ceasing to respond, the pre-order Nook STR I had suffered from that problem.  The touchscreen would become slow to respond, and eventually quit working altogether.  A power cycle generally brought it back.  I started a thread on B&N's website about this and found many others having the problem.  I did take my Nook STR back to B&N and they exchanged it.  Have had the replacement Nook ST for six weeks now and the problem hasn't returned.  So don't be spooked about buying one due to this problem - if it happens to you, exchange it.<br /><br />Going back to library books, I had to install Adobe Digital Editions onto my PC (my library had the link to download - free).  This software allows you to manage your ebooks on your Nook.<br /><br />The Nook STR is a good choice for seniors that may not have extensive computer skills.  Using the device is easy enough, but loading books from a library website (via Adobe Digital Editions) requires a few steps to complete.  If help is needed, it can be a positive relationship-building experience.<br /><br />One last plus - once you get your Nook STR, check the \\"Shop\\" tab to access B&Ns website. 0140143505|5.0|I love, love, love this charming book that reaches out and touches the bibliophile in me! Only one who has a life-long love affair with books will truly appreciate this story of the decades' long correspondence between Helene Hanff, a script reader/ writer living in New York, and Frank Doel, the manager of an antiquarian British bookshop, Marks & Co. The correspondence starts in the year 1949 and ends in 1968.<br /><br />These letters reveal two people who shared a similar sense of humor and a passion for old books. Ms. Hanff had a particular fondness for antiquarian books filled with wisdom - the works of John Donne, Samuel Pepys, Newman, Leigh Hunt, Walter Savage Landor, and many others. Despite making little money, Hanff develops such a fondness for the people running the bookshop that she sends them food parcels when she discovers England is being subjected to food rationing in the post-war period.<br /><br />My love for this story is not only because it is about a love of books, but also because it harkens back to a time when people actually took pains to correspond with each other (not that they had a choice), an art that is slowly dying out (I try my best to keep it alive by penning long letters to my parents who live on a different continent and who are blissfully oblivious to the wonders of e-mailing and social networking).<br /><br />84, Charing Cross Road resonates with me on so many levels, and is the perfect read on days when I'd like to just slow down and savor the pleasure of curling up with a light read. The movie adaptation starring Anne Bancroft and Sir Anthony Hopkins is another delight that perfectly captures the essence of the book. Also recommended:<br />[[ASIN:0140089365 Q's Legacy]]

Her personal strength is also what made me so lost in her book. I was especially grasped by reading the excerpts from her secret journal, which in this book reveals entries written between 1998 and 2007. This was probably the toughest and most difficult part of the book to read, as it gets very personal. For over thirty years, having grown up without the protective love of his father and then tragically without his mother's love, young John Sullivan is lost, adrift at sea.   A picture of depression, he can not find the strength to love anyone else and scarcely himself; he is stuck in time.    John, like his father, is a protector; he is a cop searching for a solution to the Nightingale serial- murderer case, a case which connects both his mother, his father and ultimately himself.  Across the warped, phenomenal channels of time, Johnny is miraculously connected to his father via a ham radio, on a frequency spanning the thirty years distance between them. B000063UR1|5.0|The success in the real world of the Apollo moon landings, and the success in the cinema of 2001: A SPACE ODYSSEY, led to science fiction suddenly becoming a very legitimate genre in Hollywood in the late 1960s and early 1970s.  New arenas and themes had opened up to replace the old clichs of B-grade aliens and bug-eyed monsters; now the focus was on films about the past, present, and future of mankind.  One such arena was Earth's own ecology, which, even as the 1970s began, was starting to show signs of being in trouble.  Such a fear would be translated onto film in 1971 by first-time director Douglas Trumbull, the genius special effects man behind a good deal of the success of 2001, in the deep-space ecological sci-fi film SILENT RUNNING, which became practically an instant cult classic upon its release in March 1972.    Set in a far distant future, where Earth's ecology has become a permanent train wreck, SILENT RUNNING generally takes place in the deepest and most isolated part of the Solar System onboard the American Airlines-sponsored deep-space freighter \\"Valley Forge\\", which houses three different types of what is left of Earth's greenery in geodesic domes, all of which are tended to by a very dedicated scientist (Bruce Dern).  But then comes the news that Valley Forge is being ordered back to Earth; and while that prospect pleases Dern's three human cohorts (Cliff Potts; Ron Rifkin; Jesse Vint), the fact that part of those orders also include nuking the geodesic domes and the remaining plant and animal life doesn't sit at all well with Dern.  With the help of two drone robots, Dern takes control of the 2000 foot-long freighter, leaving the one remaining domed example of Earth life attached to the main body of the ship, and putting the ship on a course that will take it around the planet Saturn and its glorious ring system and back to Earth.  Having jettisoned his human companions, who showed utter indifference to the conservation project from the beginning, he then instructs the drones in how to maintain these last remnants of Earth life.     Having achieved a lot as a special effects supervisor, first on 2001 and then on THE ANDROMEDA STRAIN in 1970-71, Trumbull managed to wangle his way into the director's chair for SILENT RUNNING, which was loosely based on an idea he himself had written a treatment for just after 2001 and which then was shaped into a screenplay by Deric Washburn, Mike Cimino (who later directed the 1978 Vietnam War epic THE DEER HUNTER), and Steven Bochco.  The elaborate nature of the story, dealing with futuristic ecology and conservation, would seem to have been hemmed in by the film's rather low budget of $1.2 million and a shooting schedule of just over a month; but Trumbull managed to pull this off by shooting all of SILENT RUNNING on location, both on the real-life, decommissioned aircraft carrier \\"Valley Forge\\" in Long Beach and at a hangar at Van Nuys Municipal Airport in the spring of 1971.  Trumbull also had the task, of course, of supervising the special effects, along with good friends Richard Yuricich and John Dykstra (both of whom he would reunite with on STAR TREK: THE MOTION PICTURE), and, given the relative paucity of the budget, they still managed to work.  As this is also a character-driven piece, it is up to Dern to largely carry the film as the ambitious botanist; and while his penchant for vituperative acting erupts at times, he does a solid job of playing somebody who is not an over-the-top nutcase.  The presence of Joan Baez's songs alongside Peter Schickele's music score may be the one thing that dates SILENT RUNNING, but it doesn't date it too seriously.    Though only a moderate box office success, due to Universal's lackluster advertising campaign (or lack thereof), SILENT RUNNING remains a thoroughly interesting film, particularly as it presages certain elements of films like the 2006 eco-documentary AN INCONVENIENT TRUTH and even certain aspects of Disney's 2008 animated sci-fi opus WALL-E.  Even with decades of special effects advancement, especially with CGI, the effects in this film still stand up to a lot of scrutiny.  But more importantly, the message being told in SILENT RUNNING remains timely to this day, and it is an important one: We are capable of saving and preserving our planet if we have the will to; but if we don't, there will come a day when there won't be anything left.

0316017930|4.0|\\"Outliers\\" is a well researched and highly pleasurable book to read. Malcolm Gladwell's method is typically to tell the story of an immensely successful person two different ways -- first, following the \\"rags to riches\\" narrative line, in which a man (and usually it is a man) makes his way up the ladder of success exclusively by dint of his own talent and effort; second, by revealing \\"the rest of the story,\\" focusing on the historical context, specific circumstances, and unseen opportunity that made it possible for the ladder to exist at all. In pitting these two kinds of narratives against each other, Gladwell redresses an imbalance in how many Americans understand success and in the second half of the book points towards social interventions that might create opportunity for more people (for example, year-long school for underprivileged students who do not have access to enrichment programs during the summer).    All of this is very good, but the insight of the book is hardly as original as Gladwell makes it out to be. Only the most unsophisticated reader actually thought before starting \\"Outliers\\" that Bill Gates (a Harvard drop-out) was truly a self-made man or that a person's cultural background has no bearing on his ability to stand up to authority (although the analysis of Korean Air plane crashes does throw this problem of culture into almost unbearable relief). In fact, Gladwell only partly addresses the question that motivated him to write the book (\\"Well, I know lots of people who are really smart and really ambitious, and they aren't worth 60 billion dollars\\" [interview in afterword]). Even his own stories, as he acknowledges, only explain how a really successful individual got his shot, not why he had the gumption to take it, or why he rather than another person nailed that shot. The part of success that individuals have CONTROL over really isn't addressed in this book. It's also part of the book's story that opportunities are often almost impossible todiscern or to seize until their moment is past, making genuine productive social intervention nigh upon impossible.    It even turns out that Gladwell wanders into a bit of a contradiction when he talks about talent, intelligence, and persistence. One vignette in this book is not a success story, the one told in the chapter \\"The Trouble with Geniuses, Part 2.\\" Here we learn about Chris Langan, a quiz show star with an impossibly high IQ who never made it big because he didn't have the social and cultural capital to finish college. But Langan actually had opportunities; he was admitted to two different colleges; he made his way onto a national TV program; his ability was widely known in his community and recognized in his family. As Gladwell explains, he lacked the \\"practical intelligence\\" to make use of those opportunities. But we never learn that \\"practical intelligence\\" isn't a form of ability (just like IQ) that is only partly a feature of one's social and family situation. The fact that it isn't commonly measured doesn't mean that it isn't part of a person's inherent make up (as Gladwell himself admits, \\"It's something, at least in part, that's in your genes\\" [101]). Thus we are returned to the story of an individual and not just of the society that creates him.    In the end, this is a worthy book, a little over-argued but valid in its effort to emphasize the role that history and culture make in creating success. True to its argument that outliers are almost always more in the mainstream than they first appear, it is not the ground-breaking work it pretends at times to be.

0312430000|4.0|I had read Atul Gawande's complications and liked his writing. And then came The Checklist Manifesto. I was intrigued - because in the business I was in at the time - conduct of examinations - the checklist is THE most important tool. Picked it up wondering what a surgeon is doing with checklists.<br /><br />Extremely interesting book - from construction to airlines, Atul explains that successful output in very complex systems involving several people and several machines is a function of having a checklist and ensuring every line item is ticked off. For instance, on a flight, when the pilot and co-pilot are taking-off they actually tick off a check list that is on the screen in front of them. This ensures the ability of complex systems to perform predictably and perform well.<br /><br />He then compares the airline pilot and the construction project manager with surgery and realizes that in surgery - a very complicated system that requires several people and several machines to work well and predictably - there are no checklists. So he introduces it and records the change in performance.<br /><br />The author reinforces the wisdom that is often manifested in organizations - that excellence is achieved through a combination of competence and extraordinary diligence. But in complex systems everyone is already competent which is why they are there. So there is a need to focus on extraordinary diligence - and checklists are a tool. \\"

B004XQO90E|4.0|Source Code (Thriller, Sci-Fi)<br />Directed by Duncan Jones<br />Starring Jake Gyllenhaal, Vera Farmiga, Michelle Monaghan and Jeffrey Wright<br /><br />Summit Entertainment   2011   93 min   Rated PG-13   Released Jul 26, 2011<br /><br />Video:<br />Video codec: MPEG-4 AVC<br />Video resolution: 1080p<br />Aspect ratio: 1.78:1<br /><br />Audio:<br />English: DTS-HD Master Audio 5.1<br />Spanish: Dolby Digital 5.1<br /><br />Subtitles:<br />English SDH, Spanish<br /><br />Disc:<br />Single 50GB Blu-ray Disc<br />Bonus View (PiP)<br /><br />The Movie 4/5<br /><br />Director Duncan Jones has had an impressive start to his career. Moon (2009) saw Sam Rockwell turn in a very good performance and Source Code has built on that success. Jones has attracted a wonderful cast and the $32 million budget allows for a more spectacular presentation. Moon reminded me of older classics such as 2001: A Space Odyssey, but Source Code feels like a modern story.<br /><br />The movie is a thriller with a strong science fiction element, but it also plays like a mystery. The opening scene shows Captain Colter Stevens (Gyllenhaal) on a train in Chicago. When he sees his reflection in the window of the train, it's not him. Christina (Monaghan), the woman in the opposite seat, calls him Sean. A few minutes later we see the train explode and Stevens wakes up alone in a chamber of some kind.<br /><br />Jones shows us everything from the viewpoint of Stevens, so we only gradually come to understand the situation. Stevens is part of an experimental project which enables him to inhabit the body of somebody else. The project is led by Dr. Rutledge (Wright) and Colleen Goodwin (Farmiga).<br /><br />They can only communicate with Stevens by using a camera and a computer screen. He's told that he must find out where the bomb is and uncover the person responsible for planting it. He can't avoid the explosion, but the information could prevent a future disaster involving the bomber. He can only inhabit the body of Sean for eight minutes at a time. So we see Stevens trying to gather information. Each time the train explodes, the pattern resets and he tries again. It's like a blend of Groundhog Day and Quantum Leap.<br /><br />We have a race against the clock to prevent further terrorism and that keeps us on the edge of our seats. There's also the chance of romance between Sean and Christina, as well as great special effects. Comedian Russell Peters provides most of the comic relief. This package has plenty to keep us hooked. At the outset I expected one of the twists to be that Sean turns out to be the bomber, but I was wrong. Couldn't Stevens simply take over the body of the bomber on his next visit once he found out who it was, or does the technology only allow you to access people who are \\"compatible\\" with you?<br /><br />Put yourself in that position for a moment. If you were a train passenger tasked with uncovering the identity of a bomber, how would you proceed? Would you be polite to total strangers as you questioned them? How about contacting the guard and explaining the situation? Whatever course of action you choose, the train will explode in eight minutes (unless you locate and disarm the bomb). That allows Jones to break the established rules somewhat. If the pattern resets each time, he can do anything. Sean can be rude, engage in criminal activity, or follow incredibly dangerous courses of action. He could even kill or be killed. That makes the movie less predictable than most.<br /><br />I won't reveal what does happen, but hopefully I've described the situation well enough for you to know whether you'll like the movie.<br /><br />The movie raises some serious themes such as how the line between right and wrong can become blurred. In a sense it also deals with the issue of slavery, just as Moon did.<br /><br />Jones has produced another interesting idea and turned it into a thrilling story. The four main actors turned in good performances and the whole thing worked well for me. I'm a fan of the whole Groundhog Day concept and enjoy Run Lola Run for the same reason. There have also been episodes of The X-Files and Star Trek Next Generation dealing with the same concept.<br /><br />Video Quality 4.5/5<br />I detected slight aliasing on a couple of overhead shots of the city, but it's only there for a second or two.
