B003Y5H5A6|5.0|\\"Captain America: The First Avenger\\" is right up there with 2008's Iron Man (and might even surpass it) as Marvel's best movie and it may well be one of the best superhero films ever. There is something about the superhero film that makes it a special genre in my heart and I am in love with the mythology of these characters. This is my first review on Amazon and I normally wouldn't go through the time to write a review but this movie is worth it. You all know the plot synopsis, so I won't repeat it here. But it is absolutely safe to say that this an excellent, fun, and amazing movie. First off, the cast is top-notch and, as Chris Evans (who is without a doubt the best of the bunch) said in a recent interview, \\"there is literally not a weak link in the chain\\". As I stated above, Chris Evans really shines, no, EXPLODES, in his role as Steve Rogers/ Captain America. I have never seen an actor embody a superhero role as greatly as Chris does. If there is one thing Marvel Studios has gotten pitch perfect in all of their films, it is the character development and casting of its lead actors, from Robert Downey Jr. who made Tony Stark one of the greatest film characters of all time to Chris Hemsworth who came out of nowhere and made the role of Thor really stand out as he embodied that character. But this isn't a review for those films, this is a review for Captain America and Evans has succeeded on all levels. I, and just about everyone else in the audience in my packed theater, fell in love with his character from when he first started out as \\"skinny Steve\\" (through the use of some pretty impressive special effects) to after the Super Soldier transformation. You can really identify with him and you can feel empathy towards his actions and his personality. Suffice to say, he has made Captain America my personal favorite superhero.   The other cast members are amazing as well. Hayley Atwell really makes a splash as British SSR officer Peggy Carter and the romance that develops between her and Steve is both believable and heartwarming. Every superhero movie has to have romance in it (it is a staple of the genre, after all) and the romance in this movie is one of the genre's best, not to mention Agent Carter is a kick-ass, strong-willed character who Atwell portrays pitch perfectly. Hugo Weaving as Cap's nemesis Johann Schmidt/ The Red Skull is both menacing (thanks to some cool and pretty impressive make-up effects) and downright evil. He stands out in all the scenes he's in and you really couldn't imagine a more perfect choice for Cap's arch enemy as Hugo portrays the character with a great German accent perfectly. That's a word that I associate with this movie a lot: perfect. I have come to believe that the word does not exist and has no meaning to it but I feel that this movie is as close as it could get to fulfiiling my expectations and more.   Now the other supporting cast members are scene-stealers in their own right. Sebastian Stan is a great friend and a great sidekick to Cap and he really does a fantastic job in his role. He isn't in the movie enough, but when he is, you can't help but like his character. His performance gets me really excited for the ensured sequels where he returns as the Winter Soldier. Other supporting cast members include Tommy Lee Jones as Col. Chester Phillips who provides a lot of great humor and good ol' American behavior as arguably the number one scene stealer and Stanley Tucci puts his best German accent to good use as the relatable and caring Dr. Abraham Erskine, who creates the Super Soldier serum. Dominic Cooper makes the role of Howard Stark is own by playing the character his own personal way and not mirroring RDJ's performance. Lastly is Toby Jones as The Red Skull's assisstant and a classic Captain America villain in his own right, Arnim Zola, who I hope to see more of in future Cap movies as well, as he gives a great performance in the movie.   Now on to the action sequences. There are fourmajor action sequences in the movie (the Heinz Krueger chase through Brooklyn, the HYDRA factory rescue mission, the high-speed train sequence, and the finale at Schmidt's HYDRA base and in the air on the giant Valkyrie warship which is by far the best, and not to mention the cool montage of Captain America and the Howling Commandoes taking out all of the HYDRA bases). Joe Johnston, the director who I am finally complementing for his superb job, stages these action sequences in a nostalgic, old-fashioned way that ironically is a breath of fresh air in today's virtual Hollywood blockbuster industry. He really was the best choice to direct this movie, as his special effects work on movies such as the Star Wars series and \\"Raiders of the Lost Ark\\", both of which are paid homage to in this movie, as well as his period movies \\"The Rocketeer\\" and \\"October Sky\\" have prepared him to make this movie. Speaking of period pieces.....  The best part to me personally about this whole movie was the production design and the cinematography. The 1940's setting is absolutely amazing and it really stands out with the coloring of the browns in Brooklyn, the grays in Europe, and the red, white, and blue of Captain America's costume. You really feel as if you are being transported to that time period and I can only hope that \\"Captain America 2\\" has flashbacks to this timeless decade. I mean, really, the period setting and filming style of this film was just knocked out of the park. The bookend scenes in the present day were done well too, especially the ending scene when Steve finds himself in modern day Times Square, New York City. This was just such a cool contrast to the rest of the movie, which may sound like a detriment to the film but it was really well done and I liked it.  In closing, I give no higher honor than to a movie like \\"Captain America: The First Avenger\\" as being my first movie review. This film was old-fashioned, exhilariting, beautiful, was filled with fine performances (especially by Chris Evans who deserves all the praise he will recieve), and was one of the most amazing superhero films I have ever seen. Steve Rogers is an inspiring character and Alan Silvestri's patriotic score that plays over the credits gives support to this and to an awesome film-going experience. I have signed up to be notified when this movie is released on home video as I would love to add it to my own personal collection. I then ordered a new copy again in 2008. Meanwhile, after years of waiting for a proper DVD release, I finally ordered the movie in '09. And as far as the CD itself, well, after the disastrous condition of my last copy, I will gladly admit, I have now learned my lesson when it comes to my precious Madonna memorabilia. These days, every time I go to reach for and/or play my favorite soundtrack, I make certain to use serious caution. Make no mistake, I still play the hell out of it, but when it comes to myself and a Madonna album, there's just no limit. The &#34;Who's That Girl&#34; movie is one of the most underappreciated movies ever made by Madonna, which, to this day, leaves me flabbergasted! The critics in 1987 ate this movie up for breakfast, constantly bad-mouthing Madonna's performance and her acting abilities in general. The very thought of critics AND fans blasting this film really annoys me! Both the album and the soundtrack hold a high level of importance in my life, so there's serious sedimental value on my part. Apparently, people can't seem to understand that the movie was MEANT to be a campy-like romantic comedy. Those who GET Madonna, GET IT, PERIOD! As an admirer of the film, I understand her comedic indication, as well as the films' intended nature. Therefore, Madonna arose to the occasion, because when it comes right down to it, &#34;Who's That Girl&#34; delivers everything it sets out toexcitement, love/romance, drama, comedy, and high-risk, on-the-edge, jam-packed action, all of which equals out to 94 minutes of amusing, enjoyable, praiseworthy entertainment!<br /><br />Who's That Girl<br /><br />The time has come for Nicole &#34;Nikki&#34; Finn's release from the Rockford Correctional Facility. After spending 4 years in Prison for being falsely-accused of murder, Nikki Finn (Madonna) is granted parole for good behavior. The parole guidelines are simple: Upon release, Nikki must report to the local Bus Station and return home to Philadelphia. With her chain-linked key (belonging to a safety-deposit box full of incriminating photos and tapes), and risqu&eacute; attire (a short black leather mini skirt, white tight tank top, 6 inch black pumps, and, of course her fire engine red lipstick), Nikki prepares to walk out the Prison a free Woman. Meanwhile, across town, Simon Worthington (John McMartin), a big shot, white-collar embezzler, gets news of Nikki's premature release from Prison, and immediately sets a plan in action, 'Operation Prisoner Shuttle.' In an attempt to conceal his role in the murder of Nikki's sweetheart, Johnny, Mr. Worthington recruits his son-in-law, tax attorney Loudon Trott (Griffin Dunne) to be Nikki's personal chauffeur, assuring Nikki's arrival to the Bus Station and off to Philadelphia. No sooner than departing from lockdown, Nikki is greeted by Mr. Trott. With time looming, and a fussy pet Cougar waiting in the wings, Loudon sets out to complete 2 errands that were asked of him: 1, see to it that the valuable endangered cougar, Murray, arrives safely to it's rightful owner, Montgomery Bell (Sir John Mills), and 2, make sure to see Nikki off to Philadelphia. But Nikki has other plans, and wastes no time picking up where she left off prior to incarceration. In an effort to avenge her innocence, and bring the 2 hit-men responsible for her lovers' timely death to justice, Nikki takes full control, sending Trott and the Cougar spiraling down a path of rampage and destruction. Insanity ensues. From shoplifting, to a near-death experience caused by a train, to a heart attack scare, and a police chase, Loudon quickly realizes that he isn't in Scarsdale anymore. After the purchase of a firearm in Harlem NY, and a stealthy stake-out, Nikki reunites herself with Raoul (Coati Mundi) and his over-sized goon Benny (Dennis Burkely), the 2 crooks who are set on keeping Nikki from clearing her name. The movie elevates with an oversufficient quantity of good ol' rock 'um sock 'um entertainment, all leading up to a romantic finale.<br /><br />Madonna's contribution to the Motion Picture Soundtrack<br /><br />During the filming of Who's That Girl, Madonna went to work simultaneously on the movie's soundtrack. The album spawned 2 chart-topping hits for her, thus dubbing the album as another 'Madonna' project. The first song released for promotion was &#34;Who's That Girl.&#34; This song sets sail tide-high, drifting along with it's bongo-beating, caribbean-esque, airy flow. In my opinion, this is one of Madonna's most passion-driven rhythmic songs to date, and my absolute favorite sentimental song ever sang/created by her (Live to Tell is my 2nd, Oh Father is 3rd, and The Look of Love stands at 4th). Next, the second song on the soundtrack, &#34;Causing a Commotion,&#34; which begins with a triple bang of drum/beat/bass, and has more of a hyperactive flow. The blender-shaken style of the instruments makes for a perfect dance hit! I fondly remember when this song made it's debut on Soul Train; a Saturday morning dance show, in '87. Causing a Commotion's jump from MTV airwaves to a predominantly African American dance show truly proves just how gifted this woman really is, and the level of universal appeal that she, a trailblazer, can obtain. Third, &#34;The Look of Love.&#34; One could easily fall into a trance due to the calm, slow, fuzzy, wildlife-sounding aura from this ballad. Madonna begins with a casual, yet remorseful, tone, while every now and again, raising her voice to get her point across. Every note hits your core repeatedly, and sends a tranquil sensation throughout your mind, body and soul. And lastly, the 4th addition to the album, &#34;Can't Stop,&#34; fully provided with a mid-pace rhythm and a whirling, robotic-talking synth. The message is direct and very clear, with 100% conviction. There is intrigue in Madonna's vocals as she exclaims: &#34;I've tried and tried to get next to you, my friends say I am blind I'll never break through.&#34; Then, boldly, &#34;But I don't give in so easily, this I know, you just wait and see.&#34; And later howls out, &#34;Don't be afraid to try, just let me love you for awhile.&#34; Madonna admits that she simply cannot stop thinking about her beau, and is determined to express just how difficult it is for her to refrain from &#34;screaming&#34; and &#34;shouting&#34; his name ever so often.<br /><br />The personal impact this movie and song have made on me<br /><br />The movie starts out with a cartoon figure of Madonna's character, Nikki Finn, introducing the film credits. The animation, song, music video and film were all inspirational to me, and played a critical role in my childhood. I was only going on 6 years old during that year, and MTV was everything, absolutely everything! And let me just say, at 6 years old, in the '80s, music videos seemed to be huge pieces of million dollar artwork in motion. Well, the Summer of 1987 was when I first viewed the music video for &#34;Who's That Girl.&#34; I was awestruck! I recall dancing around a lot, laughing, chanting the chorus section of the song in sync with Madonna, staring in amazement at the movie clips intertwined. But, the one thing that stood out above all else was the stylish animation of the character, Nikki Finn. The image of that cartoon would be my motivational fuel throughout the remainder of the '80s and well into the '90s. Basically, the fashionable illustration is why I later became interested in drawing/sketching/designing! I have been drawing since Elementary, sketching throughout Jr. High, won a few awards in High School for drawing and designing, then became interested in fashion and designing in 10th grade. In my heart, I know that I wouldn't have half of the success that I have without Who's That Girl, and I owe a fraction of my mild success to Ms. Madonna Louise Veronica Ciccone! I have the utmost respect her, and I honor her for sparking that artistic talent that I always knew I had, hidden from within.<br /><br />In Conclusion<br /><br />What ended up being a critical and commercial failure, has now turned into a cult classic. And even though the soundtrack excelled and enjoyed universal success, it's safe to guarantee that both the film and album do pretty well in sales today. Madonna has come quite far in the music/entertainment business, and she isn't budging any time soon. The very first time I laid eyes on her was in Desperately Seeking Susan, in 1985, and then in her &#34;Lucky Star&#34; and &#34;Material Girl&#34; music videos. Back then, MTV aired music videos in rotation, sometimes airing blocks of Madonna videos, in which, at that point, were an accumulation of work from 1983-1985. I was starstruck back then, and every time I witness another shift in her work, I become starstruck all over again. Madonna has remained triumphant, creating evident highlights of achievement, including selling more than 300 million albums, numerous awards, including a Golden Globe for her role in Evita, her induction into the 2008 Rock 'n' Roll Hall of Fame, all in conjunction with a colossal extent of charitable work. The list continues for this Mother of 3, but there's not much more room to continue on with her achievements. She's congenial; she's provocative; she's outrageousand over the years, we've all been witness to her evolutionfrom street-smart girlfriend, to virgin bride, a pornographic sex goddess, to a Yogi! Her mind is as celebrated equally as her body; she's as feared as she's desired; she leads while other's followa woman who set out to conquer and &#34;rule the world!&#34; ...my opinion? - Mission complete!she exclaims: &#34;I've tried and tried to get next to you, my friends say I am blind I'll never break through.&#34; Then, boldly, &#34;But I don't give in so easily, this I know, you just wait and see.&#34; And later howls out, &#34;Don't be afraid to try, just let me love you for awhile.&#34; Madonna admits that she simply cannot stop thinking about her beau, and is determined to express just how difficult it is for her to refrain from &#34;screaming&#34; and &#34;shouting&#34; his name ever so often. B003EYVXUU|2.0|When Let Me In was announced, there was backlash from fans of the original Swedish film Let the Right One In, given that the original film had just came out. It's par for the course for a foreign film to be remade so soon after its release nowadays, but director /writer Matt Reeves assured them that this would not be a typical remake. Instead, he promised that this would be a different take on the original novel by John Ajvide Lindqvist.  He had also told Lindqvist in a private conversation that he had no intention of copying the Swedish film but had fallen in love with the novel and wanted to use that. Given that the novel is significantly different from the original film, there was plenty of new material that Reeves could have chose from. For example, the character of Hakan in the novel is considerably more disturbing than anything the Swedish film showed.<br /><br />While I can't say if Reeves' film changed since he made those original comments, anyone familiar with both the novel and the Swedish film will know that this film is very much a straight up remake of the film. It follows the Swedish screenplay beat for beat, right down to elements in the Swedish film that weren't even in the original novel. There are some differences (a car crash sequence being the biggest one) but for the most part this is Tomas Alfredson's original film with minor cosmetic changes. Given how great the novel is, it's a shame that Reeves took the easy way out as opposed to looking at the original source material with fresh eyes and giving us something different. Maybe a more faithful adaptation would still have been weaker, but at least Reeves could say with honesty that he put his own stamp on the story as opposed to copying what had gone on before him.<br /><br />The film itself pretty much follows the criteria that most American remakes of foreign horror films follow nowadays (and yes, I know that it's from a British studio). I'll give Reeves credit for not making the kids high schoolers and for trying to make a  horror film for adults, but at the same time, any serious director must know that making this Twilight would be tantamount to shooting yourself in the head. So let's check go through the checklist:<br /><br />1. More violence and gore? Check. Most of the violence in the original film was restrained, here everything is cranked up to eleven. At times it's almost ridiculous. I could list numerous examples but I'd end up recapping the whole film, so one will stand for all: the finale. The original's finale is almost beautiful despite its horrific content; here, everything is loud, messy and chaotic and wouldn't seem out of place in any other horror film.<br /><br />2. Simplified plot? Check. Reeves does away with most of the supporting cast and subplots in the Swedish film. For some viewers, this has been a positive, but I felt it took away from the depth of the story. In the original film, much of the victims came across as real people and made the film more tragic. Here, they're walking blood bags and little else.<br /><br />3. Less ambiguity? Check. There's a lot of mystery in the Swedish film. You were left to draw your own conclusions. Here, Reeves spells everything out for the audience. The funny thing is, the original film really isn't that hard to follow or understand, so all the 'explaining' comes off as a lack of respect for the audience's intelligence. It's not as if the Swedish film was Synecdoche, New York.<br /><br />4. Cleaning up 'objectionable' content? Check. This is hard to explain without spoiling it, but if you've seen the Swedish film you know what I'm talking about. There's a disturbing scene that puts the entire film into a different perspective, and deals with a theme that many are uncomfortable with. Predictably (seriously, did anyone think that was going to be in this?), it's been removed.<br /><br />All in all, I really can't recommend this. At best, the film is competently made and has some decent performances. But if you've seen the Swedish film, this really doesn't offer anything different or worthwhile. It's more or less the same film, just simplified, 'cleaned up' and made gorier for mainstream audiences. If you haven't seen the Swedish film, then drop what you are doing and watch that now. If you don't want to watch the Swedish film because it's subtitled, then I'm sorry that you're missing out one of many great films from around the world.ything different or worthwhile. The lavender boy vocals of Bret Michaels are still in place but on this album the band seem to stretching out and doing different things. This becomes obvious from the title track, which is also the first tune on the album. It shows a few studio tricks being used to create a more offbeat result and it's not a bad move. As the album mooches along the band are able to go up and down the gears with ease. There aren't too many genuinely crunchy guitar numbers but this very much feels like a rock band working hard to put on a show. In fact the lightness of being here is one of the reasons you can play it in the background to good effect and even though there are 15 tracks here the album doesn't feel overlong.<br /><br />Production is as nice as you'd expect it and Ritchie Kotzen certainly adds something different in the guitar department. Something that is anathema to this bands manifesto. B000VZBCWE|5.0|I purchased this DVD after renting the Queen Rock Montreal concert via Netflix, which rekindled my love for Queen. I was a Queen fan in Jr. High and High School (saw them perform live in 1978 in Kansas City), but lost track of them in the early 1980's. My renewed interest came after seeing a rock documentary about Queen on the Ovation channel and then another documentary about Freddie Mercury: The Untold Story.  I have to say, I wasn't expecting to like this DVD. I was a fan of Queen in their early long-hair days.  I had not even listened to their music seriously since about 1980.  But I was just blown away by this concert. The band is so tight and in such great form. Freddie Mercury is incredible to watch - you can't take your eyes off him. He fills every second on stage with his presence and showmanship.     Watch the concert all the way through and then watch it again with Roger Taylor and Brian May's commentary. They take a while to get going and there are a few long silences, but they give an explanation as to why this film was not presented until recently. Something to do with having a falling out with the film makers and not having access to it all these years. The film is actually two concerts spliced together from the two concert dates in 1981 in Montreal ( you can tell by the costume changes) which they had restored and edited recently. The audio and visual quality are amazing for something this old. There is nothing like real film. Video does not even compare- you wonder, why even bother wth video when you have film that does such a superb job? The audio is so clean and the images so crisp! The later concerts do not even hold a candle to this in regards to quality. And I have to say Freddie Mercury was in rare form at the Montreal Show. Watching him jump back and forth to the piano with perfect timing is amazing. He never loses hold of the audience and keeps the group cooking away for full hour and a half. Yes, this is the concert in its entirety. Apparently this was one of the last times Queen performed with just the four guys and no additional musicians help. In the later concerts, Freddie got away from playing the piano as it kept him away from the stage and he wanted to walk around and sing and entertain the audience - and HOW!!! His voice is amazing!    Taylor and May's comments on the commentary are very endearing too as they tell little anecdotes about the band and compliment each other on drum fills and guitar solos and such during the concert. You can tell they have great respect for each other and a lot of love for Freddie Mercury and it comes out in how they notice and comment on the details; such as how nice it is to see Freddie Mercury in his muscular prime. They also mention how the band had freedome to improvise and do what they wanted during the concerts, whereas now, groups are all programmed in concert where you are not even hearing live music sometimes. I went to see a lot of rock concerts in the 1970's, and now that I think of it, they really had to work a lot harder in those days, as there just was not all the technology around that we take for granted today.    I also have to say that this concert made me realize how underappreciated Queen is now and was then. They were a truly amazing group and came up with some incredible innovations that they just don't get enough credit for. Perhaps Bohemian Rhapsody was just over played and people came to think of it as campy and jokey? And I also think a lot of people in the 1970's 80's were somewhat homophobic - and Freddie Mercury's flamboyant lifestyle on and off stage was just too wild for the maintstream? But I have to say, after watching this video and the other documentaries I saw, I realize now what an important and serious musician Freddie Mercury was and how it was his creative ideas that brought Queen to fame. Not to take anything away from the other guys - all incredible musicians in their own right who don't get enough credit for their contribution to the group's musical success. Freddie Mercury was an incredible performer who gave the audience so much more than their money's worth. You can see that he really loved what he was doing. He lived his life the way he wanted, and in doing so, as his mother says on one of the documentaries, \\"he made the whole world happy\\". There is too much snickering about his being gay and his flamboyant lifestyle. So what?  Like Oscar Wilde, he was really a man born out of his time. Oh Queen - Oh Freddie, how I miss you guys!!

0061353248|5.0|Ariely does two things in this book. First, he shows many ways in which humans consistently act irrationally: although we often don't act in line with the predictions of traditional economic theory, neither do we act chaotically. Second, he shows that we are also routinely bad at predicting how irrational our behavior will be. However, I would have liked an appendix explaining just what the term rational means.    Of course to do social science we need to abstract from how people behave to find general principles, lest we not be able to make general statements and get lost in particulars and be able to say nothing. We want to make abstractions that don't lose too much information. But human beings behaving rationally is not even a good first approximation to how people really behave. This vitiates economic theories that depend on rational utility maximizing individuals.    Chapter 1: Relativity. Having a choice that no one actually chooses can affect the decisions people make. People are more comfortable choosing an option if they have a clearly inferior option that makes it look better by comparison, since it's hard to compare very different options. So a good wingman will look similar to you but worse, rather than have a completely different body type. We will spend half an hour saving $7 off of $25 but won't spend half an hour saving $7 off of $455, even though in both cases the question ought to be are we willing to spend half an hour to save $7. Try to think about the $7 detached from a $25 purchase or a $455 purchase. Chapter 2: Anchoring. Thinking about a high number will make you more willing to pay more. People value things according to the original achors, not by how much utility they get from the thing, and thus having more options to trade can make people less happy: I can like something but irrationally sell it cheaply because of a low anchor. Chapter 3: There is an irrational difference between paying a small price and paying zero. Chapter 4: Social norms and market norms. There is tension about which norm we are using. Chapter 5: We can't predict how we will think when in an aroused state. The only way to control emotions is to remove oneself from a situation before the event horizon after which you can't go back. This applies to sex and food. Chapter 6: Procrastination and self-control. We do better when we have deadlines. Chapter 7: We irrationally value what we already have. Try to view all transactions as a nonowner. Chapter 8: We overvalue keeping options available. It can be helpful to have fewer options, yet we spend resources keeping useless options available. Chapter 9: Expectations affect experience. Chapter 10: Placebos; continuation of previous chapter. Chapter 11: When we have the chance we cheat but not as much as we could. To stay honest we need to be reminded of ethics at the moment of decision, not in a general way. Chapter 12: We steal much more when the object is not money than when it's money, even when it's just tokens that we immediately convert to money, and yet people don't expect that this is true. Chapter 13: Conclusion. Standard economic views are based on rational behavior, which this book has shown people very often do not exhibit. Therefore many conclusions of standard economic theory are baseless; they may or may not be correct, but they are not justified because their assumptions are false. Economics needs instead to be based on how people actually behave, and this is the intent of behavioral economics. Moreover, many big problems like people not saving enough are incomprehensible by standard economics.

0553801473|4.0|----------This review contains no spoilers to Dance with Dragons. ----------<br /><br />I have read and loved the world George R.R. Martin created for many years now.  I first read Game of Thrones about 10 years ago, and have been enchanted and engrossed in A Song of Ice and Fire since then.  First, I would preface my opinion by stating what I would rate each of the books in the series.  I would give 5 stars to A Game of Thrones, A Clash of Kings, and A Storm of Swords.  I would give 3 stars to A Feast for Crows.  And I give 4 stars to A Dance with Dragons.<br /><br />Dance kept me page-turning more than Feast (which felt more sluggish and departed from the original three books).  In Dance, I enjoyed the characters covered much more. There were a lot of \\"oh wow\\" moments and some definite surprises. Reading some of the reviews on Amazon, I tend to agree with a lot of the other 4-star reviews. Martin's writing is very good, and he still has the ability to surprise; but I agree that it's frustrating with how many things were left hanging and not understanding the point of some of the things that have gone on. It may be that as the rest of the story unfolds I will understand it more. Dance leaves off with a lot of uncertainties but with the feeling that the next novel is going to be huge with a lot of elements pulling together and a lot more resolution. A Storm of Swords was very much a climactic novel, and it left the period of time that Feast and Dance covers to regather and rebuild. And it builds a great deal in Dance.<br /><br />I think some of the bad reviews are people frustrated because of this building, and the desire for more resolution, not liking the idea of waiting years for more answers. My own hope is that the next book will not be so long in coming, because the tension of the story is built up high now, it seems like a lot of foundation is relaid since Storm. Things feel very ripe for an explosion of action.<br /><br />I don't really feel that some of thebad reviews I have read are deserved. The scope of the story has grown and broadened since the first book, where it initially focused on a few people, and it now is cascading out as the impact of those characters and their stories affect the state of Martin's world. The history of the world he has created is impressively rich. I won't say that I love everything that happens; I still hate what happened to Eddard for that matter. I wonder at the decisions and choices of some of the characters I've gotten close to, but they are so very human and real in my opinion.<br /><br />After reading it, I have a lot of various hopes and worries for characters, and to my mind it is a talented author who can provoke such feelings from the pages of a book.  I will be sitting and waiting for the next, as I waited for Feast and I waited for Dance.

They're more than the sum of their parts.  The series has all of the polish that one would expect from a great author: the characters are rich and round, the story is well-paced and compelling and the magic system is believable, and ingeniously implemented.  And yet there is something special about the way the book comes together.  It sparkles and shines and and hypnotizes.  There are very few books that compel me to read into the early morning hours.  This book was one of them.     Now that I'm finished with A Wise Man's Fear, I feel empty and yearning for more. B004BBDHEK|3.0|This isn't the return of Yanni as you know him!    Yanni's last album, Voices, was a significant departure from his old self, and so is this album.  However, it actually has a lot in common with Voices.    Yanni's music has always been defined by melody first, and its propulsive energy second.  In Voices, Yanni's melodies disappeared; it's hard to even hear the original melodies in some songs that are based on old instrumental favorites.  Instead, the music became accompaniment for the singers.    In Truth of Touch, the music is largely similar to Voices: it sounds for all the world like some of the songs were intended as accompaniment for singers, but the songs never came together, and Yanni released them as instrumentals!    There is a strong melodic component to some of the tracks, but it's much less prominent than you might be used to -- not as strong as Ethnicity or other recent albums, and not even approaching the prominence of melody on early albums like Chameleon Days.  In some of the songs, the melodic component is relegated to flourishes and transitions as there is a chord change.    Rhythmically, the pieces have thumping electronica percussion running throughout, and a few of the tracks sound a lot like ambient/electronica artists such as Bluestone or Sleepthief.    So this is definitely not a return to the style of Ethnicity or If I Could Tell You.  It's another change of direction for Yanni: still recognizably Yanni, but not what we're used to.  In that sense, Yanni doesn't change a thing: change is the only constant for this ever-evolving artist.

0375703764|2.0|*sigh* Full disclosure: I made it exactly 361 pages (out of the total 662) before giving up on this book. Can't say I didn't try, right?    As others have mentioned, House of Leaves is a story within a story within a story. First, there's the story of this mysterious house that is bigger on the inside than the outside. This story is told in the form of an in-depth analysis of a documentary produced about the house. Basically, the manuscript of this analysis re-tells what happens in the documentary, scene by scene, stopping frequently to examine the deeper meaning of the documentary from every angle imaginable, often going off on tangents about Greek mythology, psychology, etc, with hundreds of footnotes citing other writers' own analysis of the film. If the book stopped here, I would be giving it a higher rating. The story of the house was spooky and captivating. It would make for a great horror flick, perhaps in the style of Paranormal Activity combined with the Blair Witch Project.    Unfortunately, the author adds another layer to the story by telling it through the thoughts of a druggie tattoo-parlor employee who finds this manuscript and is reading it for himself. As he reads the manuscript of the analysis of the documentary of this house *sigh again*, he begins to go a little crazy, believing that his own apartment is changing shape with its own monster that wants to eat him. As he is reading this manuscript, he adds his own footnotes, giving his own thoughts about what the manuscript author has written, or sometimes just writing whatever happens to pop into his head. I tell you, these footnotes drove me bonkers and ultimately ruined the book for me. This character's footnotes often consist literally of page upon page of run-on, stream-of-consciousness sentences. Here's an example:    \\"It doesn't matter that she always gives me a wink and sometimes even a full throated laugh when I call her \\"Thumper\\", \\"Hi Thumper\\" \\"Bye Thumper\\" the only words I can really muster, she still really only exists for me as a strange mixture of daydream and present day edge, by which I mean something without a past or a future, an icon or idyll of sorts, for some reason forbidding to me, but seductive beyond belief and probably relief, her image feeling permanently fixed within me, but not new, more like it's been there all along, even if I know that's not true, and come last night going so far as to entwine, entangle and finally completely replace her with the (-can't write the word-) of-\\"    So... if that sounds like good reading to you, then be my guest and buy this book.    Finally, there is a group of \\"editors\\" that occasionally add their own footnotes to the whole thing. So there are footnotes by a total of three authors, each with their own font so you can tell who's who. The bulk of this book is footnotes. Seriously. Many of these footnotes span multiple pages, some are written upside down, sideways, or in reverse (so they must be viewed through a mirror), some don't even start on the same page that directed you to them, some footnotes start on a later page and you must flip backwards through the pages to read them, and then there are the footnotes that have their own footnotes. \\"Dear Sweet Jesus, when will it end?\\" is all I could think while reading this book. Some reviewers found this \\"style\\" to be novel and entertaining. I felt like I was going crazy, just like the character. Maybe that was the point? Although making me go crazy with boredom failed miserably in getting me to enjoy this story.    By the time I reached the half-way point of the book, I couldn't believe that the author actually had more to say. It seemed as if the spooky story of the shifting house was over, yet there was still half a book left! Perhaps if I had kept reading, things would have drastically improved - but I doubt it. The creepy story of the house deserves five stars. The bulk of the book deserves zero.
